::: calibre9
:::

[[paragraph and section marks]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Add a nonbreaking space]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *paragraph mark* ( ¶ ) is used when citing documents with
sequentially numbered paragraphs (e.g., declarations or complaints). The
*section mark* ( § ) is used when citing documents with numbered or
lettered sections (e.g., statutes).

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
|                 |              | [[Windows]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Mac]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[HTML]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |              |                                |                            |                             |
|                 |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
|                 |              | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+=================+==============+================================+============================+=============================+
| [¶]{.calibre20} | paragraph    | alt 0182                       | option + 7                 | ¶                           |
|                 | mark         |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9    |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::             | ::: calibre9 | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
|                 | :::          |                                |                            |                             |
+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [§]{.calibre20} | section mark | alt 0167                       | option + 6                 | §                           |
|                 |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::             | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

A paragraph mark or section mark should always be followed by a
[nonbreaking space](#calibre_link-30). The nonbreaking space acts like
glue that keeps the mark joined with the numeric reference that follows.

::: calibre9
:::

Without the nonbreaking space, the mark and the reference can end up on
separate lines or pages. This can confuse readers.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> The defendant can, under Civil Code §\
> 1782, offer a correction to buyers. But ¶\
> 17 of the agreement suggests it is required.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> The defendant can, under Civil Code\
> § 1782, offer a correction to buyers. But\
> ¶ 17 of the agreement suggests it is required.

::: calibre9
:::

If the paragraph or section reference comes at the start of a sentence,
don't use the mark --- spell out the whole word (*Section 17200 applied
to the transaction, but § 17500 did not*). In a reference to multiple
paragraphs or sections, double the mark (¶¶ or §§).

::: calibre9
:::

Citation formats for rules (e.g., the Federal Rules of Evidence, rules
of court, rules of professional conduct) don't usually call for a
section mark (*Fed. R. Evid. 801*, not *Fed. R. Evid. § 801*).

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The paragraph mark is also known as a *pilcrow*.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Your citation guide may have more to say about this topic. For
instance, the *California Style Manual* does not permit section marks
outside parenthetical citations --- you have to spell out the whole
word.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-460 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-120 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-461 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-469 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-470 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-472 .mbppagebreak}

::: calibre9
:::

[[parentheses, brackets, and braces]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Stay the course]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

A rare instance where common usage and legal usage are almost the same.
*Parentheses* are for separating citations or other asides. *Brackets*
show changes to quoted material. *Braces* are not typically used.

::: calibre9
:::

[(parentheses) \[brackets\]\
{braces}]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Some citation guides, including the *California Style Manual*, demand
brackets for parallel citations.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> "*Bertero* was not meant to discourage \[the\]\
> practice \[of pleading alternative legal theories\]." (*Crowley v.
> Katleman* (1994) 8 Cal.4th 666, 691 \[881 P.2d 1083, 34 Cal.Rptr.2d
> 386\].)

::: calibre9
:::

> [[The California Style Manual is an alternative citation system used
> in California state courts in slight preference to the Bluebook
> (though Bluebook-style citations are also
> accepted).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Why do braces still get prime real estate on keyboards? They're part
of the syntax of nearly every software-programming language. You may not
use braces, but modern life wouldn't be possible without them.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-479 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-121 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-480 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-481 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-482 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-483 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-484 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-485 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-486 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-487 .mbppagebreak}

[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Basic

::: calibre9
:::

[[hyphens and dashes]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Use them, don't confuse them]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Hyphens and dashes* look similar, but they're not interchangeable.

::: calibre9
:::

+-------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
|                   |              | [[Windows]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Mac]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[HTML]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                   |              |                                |                            |                             |
|                   |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
|                   |              | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+===================+==============+================================+============================+=============================+
| [-]{.calibre20}   | hyphen       | \-                             | \-                         | \-                          |
|                   |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9      | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::               | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+-------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [--]{.calibre20}  | en dash      | alt 0150                       | option + hyphen            | --                          |
|                   |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9      | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::               | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+-------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [---]{.calibre20} | em dash      | alt 0151                       | option +\                  | ---                         |
|                   |              |                                | shift + hyphen             |                             |
| ::: calibre9      | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   |                            | ::: calibre9                |
| :::               | :::          | :::                            | ::: calibre9               | :::                         |
|                   |              |                                | :::                        |                             |
+-------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

The *hyphen* ( - ) is the smallest of these marks. It has three uses.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  A hyphen appears at the end of a line when a word breaks onto the
    next line. These hyphens are added and removed automatically by your
    word processor's [hyphenation](#calibre_link-67) feature.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  Some multipart words are spelled with a hyphen (*topsy-turvy*,
    *cost-effective, bric-a-brac*). But a prefix is not typically
    followed with a hyphen (*nonprofit*, not *non-profit*).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  A hyphen is used in phrasal adjectives (*commercial-speech
    restriction*, *estate-planning attorney*, *law-school grades*) to
    ensure clarity. Nonprofessional writers often omit these hyphens. As
    a professional writer, you should not.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

> For instance, consider the unhyphenated phrase *five dollar bills*. Is
> *five* the quantity of *dollar bills*, or are the *bills* each worth
> *five dollars*? As written, it suggests the former. If you mean the
> latter, then you'd write *five-dollar bills*.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[No hyphen is necessary in phrasal adjectives that begin with an
> adverb ending in **-ly** (it's a **closely held company**, not a
> **closely-held company**). Nor is a hyphen necessary in multipart
> foreign terms or proper names used as adjectives (**habeas corpus
> appeal on the Third Circuit docket**, not **habeas-corpus appeal on
> the Third-Circuit docket**).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Dashes come in two sizes --- the *en dash* and the *em dash*. The em
dash ( --- ) is typically about as wide as a capital H. The en dash ( --
) is about half as wide.

::: calibre9
:::

En and em dashes are often approximated by typing two or three hyphens
in a row ( \-- or \-\-- ). Don't do that. Use real dashes.

::: calibre9
:::

The en dash has two uses.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  It indicates a range of values (*1880--1912*, *116 Cal. App. 4th
    330--39*, *Exhibits A--E*). If you open with *from*, pair it with
    *to* instead of an en dash (*from 1880 to 1912*, not *from
    1880--1912*).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  It denotes a connection or contrast between pairs of words
    (*conservative--liberal split*, *Arizona--Nevada reciprocity*,
    *Sarbanes--Oxley Act*).

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Be careful when citing a source like *Local Rule 7-3*. That gets a
hyphen, not an en dash, because it's the multipart name of a single
rule, not a range of rules.

::: calibre9
:::

The em dash is used to make a break between parts of a sentence. Use it
when a comma is too weak, but a colon, semicolon, or pair of parentheses
is too strong. The em dash puts a nice pause in the text --- and it is
underused in legal writing.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Don't use a slash ( / ) where an en dash is correct.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Even though the en dash is used for joint authors (*Sarbanes--Oxley
Act*), use a hyphen for compound names. If the children of Sarbanes and
Oxley married, they'd be known as *Mr. & Mrs. Sarbanes-Oxley* (with a
hyphen), not *Mr. & Mrs. Sarbanes--Oxley* (with an en dash).

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Em and en dashes are typically set flush against the surrounding text.
Some fonts include a little white space around the em dash; some don't.
If your em dashes look like they're being crushed, it's fine to add
[word spaces](#calibre_link-29) before and after.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ An en dash makes an acceptable minus sign in spreadsheets or
mathematical expressions. (See also [math symbols](#calibre_link-37).)

::: calibre9
:::

➤ *Em* and *en* refer to units of typographic measurement, not to the
letters M and N. In a traditional metal font, the em was the vertical
distance from the top of a piece of type to the bottom. The en was half
the size of the em. Originally, the width of the em and en dashes
corresponded to these units. In today's digital fonts, they run
narrower.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000016.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Basic

::: calibre9
:::

[[trademark and copyright symbols]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Don't use alphabetic substitutes]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Your keyboard includes a *trademark symbol* ( ™ ), a *registered
trademark symbol* ( ® ), and a *copyright symbol* ( © ).

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
|                 |              | [[Windows]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Mac]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[HTML]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |              |                                |                            |                             |
|                 |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
|                 |              | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+=================+==============+================================+============================+=============================+
| [™]{.calibre20} | trademark    | alt 0153                       | option + 2                 | ™                           |
|                 |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::             | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [®]{.calibre20} | registered   | alt 0174                       | option + r                 | ®                           |
|                 | trademark    |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9    |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::             | ::: calibre9 | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
|                 | :::          |                                |                            |                             |
+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [©]{.calibre20} | copyright    | alt 0169                       | option + g                 | ©                           |
|                 |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::             | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+-----------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

Use these symbols when you need them. Don't use alphabetic
approximations like *(TM)* or *(c)*.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
(c) 2010 MegaCorp (TM)\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
© 2010 MegaCorp™]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Trademark symbols are set as superscripts --- smaller characters
positioned above the baseline of the text (*Roxy's Pizza*™, *Caring Is
Our Business*®). If you use proper trademark symbols, they'll appear at
the right size and height. No space is needed between the text and the
trademark symbol.

::: calibre9
:::

Copyright symbols appear in line with the text (*© 1999*). Use a
[nonbreaking space](#calibre_link-30) between the copyright symbol and
the year to ensure the two don't end up on different lines or pages.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> This instructional goatherding video is ©\
> 2010 MegaCorp Inc.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> This instructional goatherding video is\
> © 2010 MegaCorp Inc.

::: calibre9
:::

Must you put a word space after the copyright symbol? No, but
semantically, it makes good sense. The © directly replaces the word
*copyright*, so it should be spaced like any other word. Trademark
symbols, on the other hand, are more like little barnacles of legalese
--- immovable but mostly ignored. To think of it another way, if you
were reading the phrase "© 2010 MegaCorp™" aloud, you'd probably
pronounce the © but not the ™.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Some word processors automatically substitute a symbol when you type
(TM), (R), or (c). Be cautious with this feature, because citations like
*Fed. R. Civ. P. 12(c)* can be silently converted to *Fed. R. Civ. P.
12*©. The spelling checker won't detect this error and it's easy to
overlook while editing.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ *Copyright © 2010* is redundant. Word or symbol --- not both.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ To dispel an urban legend that persists among many civilians, and more
than a few lawyers: with respect to newly created works, the copyright
symbol has no magic powers. Putting a copyright symbol on something you
made does not grant you a copyright. Nor does failing to use it deprive
you of a copyright.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ I always use the nonitalicized versions of these marks, even if the
adjacent text is italicized. I think it looks better. But that's a
preference, not a rule.

::: calibre9
:::

::: calibre9

[[ampersands]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Use sparingly]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *ampersand* is typographic shorthand for the word *and*. The
ampersand is halfway between a [ligature](#calibre_link-38) and a
contraction, a stylized depiction of the Latin word *et*.

::: calibre9
:::

The ampersand is one of the jauntiest characters. Font designers often
use it as an opportunity to show off. Traditional ampersands take the
shape of the word *et*. Modern ampersands are more stylized.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000017.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Ampersands are completely correct when they're part of a proper name
(*Fromage & Cracotte LLP*) or an official citation format (*Bus. & Prof.
Code § 17200*).

::: calibre9
:::

Past that, ampersands should be handled like any other contraction: the
more formal the document, the more sparingly they should be used. Here
and there, but not everywhere.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-542 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::: calibre9
:::

[[ellipses]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Avoid using periods and spaces]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

An *ellipsis* (plural *ellipses*) is a sequence of three dots used to
indicate an omission in quoted material.

::: calibre9
:::

+--------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
|              |              | [[Windows]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Mac]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[HTML]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|              |              |                                |                            |                             |
|              |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
|              |              | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+==============+==============+================================+============================+=============================+
| ...          | ellipsis     | alt 0133                       | option +\                  | ...                         |
|              |              |                                | semicolon                  |                             |
| ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   |                            | ::: calibre9                |
| :::          | :::          | :::                            | ::: calibre9               | :::                         |
|              |              |                                | :::                        |                             |
+--------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

The ellipsis is frequently approximated by typing three periods in a
row, which puts the dots too close together, or three periods with
spaces in between, which puts the dots too far apart.

::: calibre9
:::

So use the ellipsis character, not the approximations.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
from a \... to z\
[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
from a . . . to z\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
from a ... to z]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Should you put [word spaces](#calibre_link-29) around an ellipsis? As
with the [em dash](#calibre_link-20), that's up to you. Typically you'll
want spaces before and after, but if that looks odd, you can take them
out. If there's text on only one side of the ellipsis, use a
[nonbreaking space](#calibre_link-30) on that side so the ellipsis
doesn't get separated from the text.

::: calibre9
:::

[Bluebook ellipses]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

*Bluebook* rule 5.3 (19th ed. 2010) demands ellipses that look like
periods with word spaces between them and around them.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
imperative to . . . courts]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

The problem with using periods and word spaces is that it invites your
word processor to break the ellipsis across different lines or pages,
like so:

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
imperative to . .\
. courts]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

To keep the dots together, make a *Bluebook* ellipsis out of three
periods with [nonbreaking spaces](#calibre_link-30) in between. Also use
nonbreaking spaces on the ends unless there's text on both sides. This
ensures the periods will behave like a single unit of punctuation.

::: calibre9
:::

When the *Bluebook* calls for a four-dot sequence, build it the same way
as a three-dot ellipsis --- as four periods with nonbreaking spaces in
between.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Keep in mind that the Bluebook is a guide to citation format, not
> typography. If your document doesn't contain citations that need to be
> in Bluebook format, you don't have to follow the Bluebook's
> typographic rules.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ I sometimes wonder whether the zigzagging illogic of the *Bluebook* is
calculated to protect its franchise --- after all, if legal citation
were distilled to a few simple rules, no one would need the *Bluebook*.
Its subtitle ---"A Uniform System of Citation"--- compresses a lot of
dark humor into five words.

::: calibre9
:::

The problem with the *Bluebook*'s four-dot-sequence rules is that they
use the same visual mark --- four periods separated by spaces --- to
denote at least four distinct conditions. Namely: a deletion before a
sentence-ending period (rule 5.3(b)(iii)); a sentence-ending period
before a deletion (rule 5.3(b)(v)); a deletion both at the end and after
the end of a sentence (rule 5.3(b)(vi)); and a deletion of one or more
paragraphs (rule 5.1(a)(iii)). This invites ambiguity. When readers come
upon a four-dot sequence, how do they know what it signifies? It may not
be clear from context. Proper ellipses would help distinguish these
conditions.

::: calibre9
:::

> [["\[The Bluebook\] is a monstrous growth. ... I have not read the
> nineteenth edition. I have dipped into it, much as one might dip one's
> toes in a pail of freezing water."\
> --- Judge Richard A. Posner, The Bluebook Blues, 120 Yale L.J. 850,
> 851--852 (2011).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-565 .mbppagebreak}
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-566 .mbppagebreak}

[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[signature lines]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Type a sequence of underscores]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

A *signature line* is a horizontal line aligned with adjacent text.

::: calibre9
:::

Typography purists avoid accomplishing anything by holding down keys on
the keyboard. But in this case it's the simplest solution. To make a
signature line, hold down the underscore key (shift + hyphen) until you
get the length you need.

::: calibre9
:::

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

::: calibre9
:::

A rival school of thought suggests you should type a series of [word
spaces](#calibre_link-29) and format them with
[underlining](#calibre_link-40).

::: calibre9
:::

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

::: calibre9
:::

Same thing, right? Not quite. There are three good reasons to prefer
underscores to underlined word spaces.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  If you need to quickly rid a document of
    [underlining](#calibre_link-40), you might want to select all the
    text and then uncheck the underlining option. But this will wreck
    signature lines made out of underlined word spaces --- they will
    disappear.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  If you need to quickly ensure you only have [one space between
    sentences](#calibre_link-15), you might want to search for and
    replace any double spaces. But this will also wreck signature lines
    made out of word spaces --- by partially deleting them.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Underscore characters don't depend on formatting, so they will look
    the same no matter where they're copied and pasted. Underlined word
    spaces may not.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

You should depart from this rule only if the font you're using has an
underscore character that doesn't form a solid line when used in
sequence. That's how underscores are supposed to work, but some fonts
are defective. Sometimes the gaps only appear on screen, so print a test
page. If the gaps appear in print too, use underlined word spaces
instead.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[To make a signature block that won't break across pages, see [keep
> lines together](#calibre_link-73).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-633 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-634 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-635 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-636 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-637 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-638 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-639 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[apostrophes]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Make sure they're curly and point downward]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *apostrophe* has two functions we all remember from sixth-grade
English class.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  An apostrophe indicates the possessive case (*Jessica's bagel*)*.*

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  In contractions, an apostrophe takes the place of letters or numbers
    that have been removed (*is not* becomes *isn't*, *Patent No.
    5,269,211* becomes *'211*).

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Apostrophes always point downward. If the smart-quote feature of your
word processor is on (see [straight and curly
quotes](#calibre_link-14)), then type an apostrophe with the same key
you use to type a straight single quote ( \' ). Your word processor will
convert this character to a curly apostrophe ( ' ). Or you can type an
apostrophe directly, using the same key as a closing single quote.

::: calibre9
:::

+------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
|                  |              | [[Windows]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Mac]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[HTML]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                  |              |                                |                            |                             |
|                  |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
|                  |              | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+==================+==============+================================+============================+=============================+
| [\']{.calibre20} | straight     | \'                             | \'                         | \'                          |
|                  | single quote |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9     |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::              | ::: calibre9 | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
|                  | :::          |                                |                            |                             |
+------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [']{.calibre20}  | apostrophe   | alt 0146                       | option +\                  | '                           |
|                  | (same as     |                                | shift + \]                 |                             |
| ::: calibre9     | closing      | ::: calibre9                   |                            | ::: calibre9                |
| :::              | single       | :::                            | ::: calibre9               | :::                         |
|                  | quote)       |                                | :::                        |                             |
|                  |              |                                |                            |                             |
|                  | ::: calibre9 |                                |                            |                             |
|                  | :::          |                                |                            |                             |
+------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

Text imported from a plain-text source (e.g., a deposition transcript or
e-mail) may not have its apostrophes converted to curly apostrophes. To
fix this, use the search-and-replace technique in [straight and curly
quotes](#calibre_link-14).

::: calibre9
:::

Wrinkles arise when an apostrophe is used at the beginning of a word
(again, assuming your smart-quote feature is on). If you type the
phrase:

::: calibre9
:::

[In the \'60s, rock \'n\' roll]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

This will be displayed as:

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
In the '60s, rock 'n' roll]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[No, you don't need an apostrophe before the **s** in
> **'60s**.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The problem here is that the characters in front of *60s* and *n'*
aren't apostrophes --- they're opening single quotes. They point upward.
What you need is an apostrophe in place of each sequence of omitted
letters, so the result looks like this:

::: calibre9
:::

[[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
In the '60s, rock 'n' roll]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

To get this result, you have two choices. You can manually delete the
incorrect marks and type apostrophes directly. Or you can type straight
single quotes twice:

::: calibre9
:::

[In the \'\'60s, rock \'\'n\' roll]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

These will be displayed as:

::: calibre9
:::

[In the ''60s, rock ''n' roll]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Then you can delete the unneeded opening single quotes, getting you back
to:

::: calibre9
:::

[[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
In the '60s, rock 'n' roll]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Word has its own apostrophe shortcut --- hold down the control key and
type the straight single quote twice.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If you're using Hawaiian spellings of Hawaiian words, look out. Those
apostrophe-like characters aren't apostrophes --- they're *okinas*. The
okina is a letter in the Hawaiian alphabet that doesn't exist in
English. Okinas point upward, so use an opening single quote as your
okina, not an apostrophe. If the okina appears in the middle of a word,
your word processor will incorrectly insert an apostrophe.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
Hawai'i O'ahu's\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
Hawai'i O'ahu's]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Alternatively, you can omit the okinas. Anglicized spellings of Hawaiian
words are almost always acceptable.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
Hawaii Oahu's]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The okina is a *glottal stop*. In English, the glottal stop is heard
before some vowels, like in the middle of the word *uh-oh*.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-640 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-128 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-641 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-642 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-643 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-644 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-645 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-646 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-647 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-648 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-649 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-650 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-651 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-652 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-653 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-654 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-655 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-656 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-657 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-658 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-659 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-660 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-661 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-662 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-663 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-664 .mbppagebreak}

[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[accented characters]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Don't ignore them]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

I assume you're writing in American English, but you might still
encounter *accented characters* in foreign words. Foreign words arise in
two situations:

::: calibre9
:::

1.  In proper names, like people and places (*Albrecht Dürer, François
    Truffaut, Plácido Domingo*). In names, accented characters must
    always appear accurately. Otherwise, the name is misspelled.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  In loanwords used in American English. Some of these words have
    become naturalized citizens and should be spelled without accents
    (*naive* for *naïve*, *melee* for *mêlée*, *coupe* for *coupé*).
    Others have not and should not (*cause célèbre*, *piña colada*,
    *Götterdämmerung*). Check a dictionary or usage guide.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

This book has a [table of the most common accented
characters](#calibre_link-3) and how to type them.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Proper names are not italicized, but loanwords sometimes are,
depending on their degree of assimilation. Again, check a dictionary or
usage guide.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The German letter *Eszett* ( ß ) is technically a
[ligature](#calibre_link-38), not an accented character: it takes the
place of *ss* in a word like *Straße*. Unlike ligatures in English, its
use in German is not discretionary --- Germany has adopted rules for
when it must appear and when it must not. (To the surprise of no one.)
Switzerland, meanwhile, disregards the Eszett, and just uses ss. And no
one uses the Eszett with [all caps](#calibre_link-45). It's best to
follow Switzerland's lead and ignore it. Even if you place the Eszett
correctly, it's less common to English speakers than the usual accented
characters, and it can easily be mistaken for a letter *B* or a Greek
beta ( β ). If you really must type one, on Windows it's alt 0223; on
the Mac it's option + s.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-667 .mbppagebreak}
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-129 .calibre}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-687 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-688 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-689 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-690 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-691 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-692 .mbppagebreak}

[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced

::: calibre9
:::

[[foot and inch marks]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Use straight quotes, not curly]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Foot and inch marks* --- also known as minute and second marks or prime
and double prime marks, depending on what they're labeling --- are not
curly. Use straight quotes for these marks.

::: calibre9
:::

+------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
|                  |              | [[Windows]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Mac]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[HTML]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                  |              |                                |                            |                             |
|                  |              | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
|                  |              | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+==================+==============+================================+============================+=============================+
| [\']{.calibre20} | foot mark    | \'                             | \'                         | \'                          |
|                  |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9     | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::              | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+
| [\"]{.calibre20} | inch mark    | \"                             | \"                         | \"                          |
|                  |              |                                |                            |                             |
| ::: calibre9     | ::: calibre9 | ::: calibre9                   | ::: calibre9               | ::: calibre9                |
| :::              | :::          | :::                            | :::                        | :::                         |
+------------------+--------------+--------------------------------+----------------------------+-----------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

"But back in [straight and curly quotes](#calibre_link-14), you told me
to turn on smart quotes. So now, when I try to type foot and inch marks,
they come out curly, not straight." A fair point.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to override smart quotes**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Type the foot or inch mark, which will come out curly,
> and then press control + z (undo). The curly mark will become
> straight.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| No special action necessary. After numbers,
> WordPerfect overrides smart quotes and inserts straight
> quotes.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Edit›* ➤ *‹Special Characters›* ➤ *‹Punctuation›*. In
> the search box at the bottom, enter *‹apostrophe›* for the foot mark
> or *‹quotation mark›* for the inch mark and press enter. The mark will
> be highlighted in the window above. Drag the mark into your text.
> Don't double-click, or it will be inserted as a curly
> mark.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Typography purists would point out that proper foot and inch marks have
a slight northeast-to-southwest slope to them. True, but those
characters aren't available in most fonts. So straight quotes are the
most reliable tools for the job.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
--118° 19' 36.9"\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
--118° 19\' 36.9\"]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

You can italicize the straight quotes if you want an even better
approximation of sloped foot and inch marks.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[BONUS POINTS:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
--118° 19*′* 36.9*″*]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Tread carefully if foot and inch marks appear within quoted material.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
"She's 6' 10"."\
[[HOPELESS:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
\"She\'s 6' 10".\"\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
"She's 6\' 10\"."]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ A [nonbreaking space](#calibre_link-30) in the middle of a
foot-and-inch measurement will prevent it from splitting onto the next
line. So instead of *6\'\
10\"*, you'll get *6\' 10\"*.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-695 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-130 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-696 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-697 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-698 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-699 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-700 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-701 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-702 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-703 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-704 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-705 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-706 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-707 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-708 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-709 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-710 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-711 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-712 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-713 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-714 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-715 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-716 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-717 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-718 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-719 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-720 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-721 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-722 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-723 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[white-space characters]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[For control and predictability]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

You're now familiar with the essential alphabetic, numeric, and symbol
characters. We turn to the frequently overlooked *white-space
characters* --- the keyboard characters that put blank space between
point A and point B.

::: calibre9
:::

Why are they overlooked? For one thing, they're invisible.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[If I have to work on a document from an outside source, one of the
> first things I do is display white-space characters, to uncover
> lurking horrors.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to display white-space characters**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹¶›* button on the standard toolbar (or control +
> shift + 8)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Home›* ➤ *‹Paragraph›* panel ➤ *‹¶›* button
> (or control + shift + 8)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Show ¶›* (or control + shift +
> F3)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Show Invisibles›* (or command + shift +
> i)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

As you learn to use white-space characters, you'll find it helpful to
make them visible so you can verify that you're typing them correctly
and that they're having the intended effect. After you're proficient,
you'll find it useful to see the white-space characters while diagnosing
formatting problems.

::: calibre9
:::

There are six important white-space characters: the [word
space](#calibre_link-29), the [nonbreaking space](#calibre_link-30), the
[tab](#calibre_link-31), the [hard line break](#calibre_link-32), the
[carriage return](#calibre_link-33), and the [hard page
break](#calibre_link-34). Each white-space character has a distinct
function. Use the right tool for the job.

::: calibre9
:::

"But if all white space looks the same when printed, why should I care?"
Two reasons: *control* and *predictability*.

::: calibre9
:::

*Control* means you get the intended result with the minimum keystrokes.
Suppose you need a paragraph to start at the top of the next page. What
do you do? If you use a hard page break rather than a sequence of
carriage returns, you get the job done with one keystroke.

::: calibre9
:::

*Predictability* means that as you edit and reformat, you'll always get
the same result. If you approximate a hard page break with carriage
returns, at some point in your editing, your text will reflow and you'll
have a large, unexpected gap where you intended a page break. Then
you'll have a new problem to diagnose and fix. But a hard page break
will always do the right thing.

::: calibre9
:::

The time you invest in learning how to use white-space characters will
be paid back in layouts that come together faster and require less
maintenance.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-724 .mbppagebreak}

[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[word spaces]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Exactly one at a time]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *word space* has exactly one purpose: to create horizontal clearance
between two words. Likewise, the space bar has exactly one purpose: to
insert a single word space.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| word space      | space bar                   | space bar                          | space bar                    |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
| :::             | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

Don't use the space bar as a general-purpose white-space dispenser by
holding it down and watching the cursor glide across the screen. Though
it may be a calming sight, it leads to anguish when formatting the
document. Use exactly one word space at a time.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-754 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::
::::::::::
:::::::::::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-132 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-755 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-756 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-757 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-758 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-759 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-760 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-761 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-762 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-764 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-780 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-781 .mbppagebreak}
