[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[nonbreaking spaces]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Prevent awkward breaks]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Your word processor assumes that a word space marks a safe place to flow
text onto a new line or page. A *nonbreaking space* is the same width as
a word space, but it prevents the text from flowing to a new line or
page. It's like invisible glue between the words on either side.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| nonbreaking     | control + shift +\          | control + space bar                | option + shift + space bar   |
| space           | space bar                   |                                    |                              |
|                 |                             | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | :::                                | :::                          |
| :::             | :::                         |                                    |                              |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

Put a nonbreaking space before any numeric or alphabetic reference to
prevent awkward breaks. Recall this example from [paragraph and section
marks](#calibre_link-18):

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> The defendant can, under Civil Code §\
> 1782, offer a correction to buyers. But ¶\
> 17 of the agreement suggests it is required.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> The defendant can, under Civil Code\
> § 1782, offer a correction to buyers. But\
> ¶ 17 of the agreement suggests it is required.

::: calibre9
:::

In the top example, normal word spaces come after the § and ¶ symbols,
and the numeric references incorrectly appear on the next line.

::: calibre9
:::

In the bottom example, nonbreaking spaces come after the § and ¶
symbols. This time, the symbols and the numeric references stay
together.

::: calibre9
:::

Use nonbreaking spaces after other abbreviated reference marks (*Ex.
A*, *Fig. 23*), after copyright symbols (see [trademark and copyright
symbols](#calibre_link-21)), and between the dots
in *Bluebook*-compliant [ellipses](#calibre_link-23).

::: calibre9
:::

In citations, use your judgment. In the citation *Fed. R. Evid. 702*,
you can put a nonbreaking space before the *702* so it won't get
separated from *Evid*. But certain citation formats, like
the *California Style Manual*, don't use spaces in the abbreviated name
of the source (*116 Cal.App.4th 602*). In those cases, the nonbreaking
space can cause more problems than it solves, because it creates a
large, unbreakable chunk of letters.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-784 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-133 .calibre}
\

::: calibre9
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-785 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[tabs and tab stops]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[For horizontal space in the middle of a line]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

On typewriters, the *tab* key moved the carriage to a fixed horizontal
position, marked with a *tab stop*. This allowed typists to create
columns of text or numbers, also known as tabular layouts (hence the
name *tab*).

::: calibre9
:::

Tabs and tab stops still work the same way. A tab stop marks a location;
typing a tab moves the cursor to that location.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| tab             | tab                         | tab                                | tab                          |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
| :::             | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

These days, the tab is used only for inserting horizontal space in the
middle of a line. If you need horizontal space at the beginning of a
paragraph, adjust the [first-line indent](#calibre_link-61). For a true
tabular layout, use a [table](#calibre_link-70), not tabs.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WordPerfect displays its default tab stops on its ruler. Word and
> Pages do not (for no good reason). In Word and Pages, be aware that
> adding your own tab stops wipes out the default ones. So you'll
> usually want to set tab stops before inserting tabs in the
> text.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The tab is not as vital as it once was, but word processors still
shortchange its capabilities. A new word-processing document has default
tab stops every half inch. These default tab stops exist so that
something happens when you type a tab in the new document. But this
default behavior also suggests that what the tab key does is move the
cursor a half inch at a time. Not true.

::: calibre9
:::

To get the most out of tabs, you should set your own tab stops. Avoid
relying on the default tab stops --- they undermine the goals of control
and predictability. As with [word spaces](#calibre_link-29), also avoid
using sequences of tabs to move the cursor around the screen.

::: calibre9
:::

To see your tab stops, display the ruler.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to display the ruler]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Ruler›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Show/Hide›* panel ➤
> *‹Ruler›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2010** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Show›* panel ➤ *‹Ruler›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Ruler›*, or type alt + shift +
> F3]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Show Rulers›*, or type command +
> r]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

With the ruler visible, you can edit your tab stops.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to work with tab stops**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  To insert a new tab stop, click in the ruler where you want the tab
    stop.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  To move a tab stop, click and drag it in the ruler.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  To change a tab stop from one kind to another, double-click it in
    the ruler. In WordPerfect and Pages, you can also right-click it.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
4.  To remove a tab stop, drag it off the ruler.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

The usual tab stop is a left tab stop, meaning text under the tab stop
aligns to its left edges. Word processors also offer center and right
tab stops, having the expected effects, as well as decimal tab stops
that align columns of numbers along their decimal points.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000018.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Many layout tasks traditionally handled by tabs are now better
> handled by tables. For instance, this illustration of tab stops was
> made with tabs, but it would've been easier to use a four-column,
> four-row table.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Use the proper tab stop for the job. For instance, don't use a center
tab stop to line up decimal numbers. A right tab can be useful in a
document footer to put two pieces of text (e.g., the document title and
the page number) at opposite ends of the same line.

::: calibre9
:::

Tabs are used in [bulleted and numbered lists](#calibre_link-69) to
separate the bullet or number from the text. Tabs are also used in
automatically generated tables of contents and tables of authorities to
put the page numbers at the right edge of the table.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Despite their names, automatic tables of contents and tables of
> authorities are not made with true word-processor tables. If you want
> to edit them, you need to be comfortable adjusting tab
> stops.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Tabs and tab stops have their place, but in most cases they act as a
less-capable alternative to a [table](#calibre_link-70). Use tabs and
tab stops if your formatting task is truly simple. If not, upgrade to a
table.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ You can fill the space in front of a tab (for instance, with periods
or underscores) by using a *tab leader*. Don't approximate this effect
by typing a hundred periods or underscores manually in front of a tab.
That will lead to unpleasant consequences.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-786 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-134 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-787 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-788 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[hard line breaks]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Same paragraph, different line]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *hard line break* moves the next word to the beginning of a new line
without starting a new paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| hard line break | shift + enter               | control + shift + L                | shift + enter                |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
| :::             | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

A hard line break can help control text flow when a [carriage
return](#calibre_link-33) won't work. For instance, this
[heading](#calibre_link-47) breaks awkwardly:

::: calibre9
:::

**IV. The defendant is entitled to judgment as a matter of law.**

::: calibre9
:::

Suppose you want the line to break after *judgment* so the first line
ends in a more logical place and the two lines are better balanced. If
you use a [carriage return](#calibre_link-33), you'll get:

::: calibre9
:::

[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
**IV. The defendant is entitled to judgment**

::: calibre9
:::

**V. as a matter of law.**

::: calibre9
:::

Not what you want. Instead, put a hard line break after judgment:

::: calibre9
:::

[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
**IV. The defendant is entitled to judgment\
as a matter of law.**

::: calibre9
:::

Hard line breaks are also useful for separating the lines of an address
(for instance, on [letterhead](#calibre_link-86) or a [caption
page](#calibre_link-83)). See [centered text](#calibre_link-59) for
another example of the hard line break in use.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-789 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-135 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-790 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-791 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-792 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[carriage returns]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Only when you want a new paragraph]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

On manual typewriters, the *carriage* was the cylinder that held the
paper and scooted leftward as you typed. At the end of each line, you'd
push a lever to move the carriage to the beginning of the next line. On
electric typewriters, this lever became the *carriage return* key, which
you'd use at the end of each line.

::: calibre9
:::

The terminology has stayed with us, but on a word processor, you only
use a carriage return to start a new paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| carriage return | enter                       | enter                              | enter                        |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
| :::             | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

As with the [word space](#calibre_link-29), use only one carriage return
at a time. It's common to see multiple carriage returns used to add
vertical space between paragraphs. Bad idea. If you want vertical space
after a paragraph, use [space between paragraphs](#calibre_link-62).

::: calibre9
:::

"But it's so much easier to type two carriage returns." I know. But in
long, structured documents, extra carriage returns create unpredictable
consequences as the document is edited. Whatever time you save with the
shortcut will cost you later.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[In e-mail, it's fine to use multiple carriage returns to add
> vertical space, because there's no other way to do
> it.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

What if you get a document that's already littered with double carriage
returns? Search-and-replace works with [white-space
characters](#calibre_link-28) too.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to replace double carriage returns**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003 & 2007** \| *‹Find and Replace›* (control + f) ➤
> *‹Replace›* ➤ *‹More›*. Use the *‹Special›* menu to put two
> *‹Paragraph Marks›* in the *‹Find what›* box, and one *‹Paragraph
> Mark›* in the *‹Replace with›* box. (Careful: you don't want the
> *‹Paragraph Character›*, which denotes the literal ¶ symbol.) Click
> *‹Replace All›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2010** \| Open *‹Find and Replace›* with control + g, then
> continue as described above.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Find and Replace›* (control + f) ➤ *‹Match›* ➤
> *‹Codes›*. Scroll down to the code *‹HRt›*. (*‹HRt›* is short for
> "hard return," WordPerfect's term for a carriage return.) Put two
> *‹HRt›*s in the *‹Replace›* box and one in the *‹Replace with›* box.
> Click *‹Replace All›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Find & Replace›* (command + f) ➤ *‹Advanced›*. Use the
> *‹Insert›* popup menu next to the *‹Find›* box to insert two
> *‹Paragraph Breaks›*. Use the *‹Insert›* popup menu next to the
> *‹Replace›* box to insert one *‹Paragraph Break›*. (There's also a
> character on the *‹Insert›* popup menu called *‹Carriage Return›*.
> Don't use this --- it won't have the intended effect.) Click *‹Replace
> All›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-793 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-136 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-794 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-795 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-796 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-797 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[hard page breaks]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Move to the top of the next page]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *hard page break* puts the next word at the top of a new page.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| hard page break | control + enter             | control + enter                    | control +\                   |
|                 |                             |                                    | num-pad enter                |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       |                              |
| :::             | :::                         | :::                                | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 |                             |                                    | :::                          |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

To move text to the next page, use one hard page break, not multiple
[carriage returns](#calibre_link-33). If you use carriage returns, your
document will become impossible to edit --- as soon as you change
anything before the page break, the text will go out of alignment. The
hard page break guarantees consistent behavior.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to insert a hard page break (alternate method)]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Break›* ➤ *‹Page Break›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Page Layout›* ➤ *‹Page Setup›* panel ➤
> *‹Breaks›* ➤ *‹Page›*, or *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Pages›* panel ➤ *‹Page
> Break›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹New Page›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Page Break›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If you use a hard page break, don't fill the blank lines on the
previous page with slashes. It's a meaningless and obsolete ritual.

::: calibre9
:::

/ /\
/ /\
/ /

::: calibre9
:::

I theorize that this habit descended from some early digital
word-processing system that used two slashes to signal a new line. I
don't know for sure. It doesn't matter. What matters is that you stop.
The slashes look bad and they create needless cleanup work every time
you edit the document and the location of the page break changes.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Hard page breaks can be built into paragraph styles to ensure that
classes of paragraphs (e.g., major section headings) always start at the
top of a new page. See [page break before](#calibre_link-75).

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-798 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-137 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-799 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-800 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-801 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-802 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-803 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[nonbreaking hyphens]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Hyphens that act like nonbreaking spaces]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Your word processor assumes that any hyphen marks a safe place to flow
the text onto a new line or page. Similar to the [nonbreaking
space](#calibre_link-30), the *nonbreaking hyphen* looks identical to a
hyphen but will not be used as a place for a line break or page break.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| nonbreaking     | control +\                  | control +\                         | see below                    |
| hyphen          | shift + hyphen              | hyphen                             |                              |
|                 |                             |                                    | ::: calibre9                 |
| ::: calibre9    | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | :::                          |
| :::             | :::                         | :::                                |                              |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

To what end? A nonbreaking hyphen guards against an awkward line break
in a citation with an internal hyphen.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> The meeting required by Local Rule 7-\
> 3 was scheduled for July 15, 2009.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> The meeting required by Local Rule\
> 7-3 was scheduled for July 15, 2009.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to insert a nonbreaking hyphen in pages]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [Pages \| *‹Edit›* ➤ *‹Special Characters›*. Using the box at the
> bottom of the window, search for "non-breaking hyphen," which will
> bring up the character.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-804 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-138 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-805 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-806 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-807 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-808 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-809 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-810 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[optional hyphens]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Mark a hyphenation location]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *optional hyphen* is usually invisible. The optional hyphen marks
where a word should be hyphenated if the word lands at the end of a
line. You can put multiple optional hyphens in a word.

::: calibre9
:::

Why would you want to do this? Some words bedevil hyphenation engines.
For instance, *TrueType* will often get hyphenated as *Tru-\
eType*. To prevent this, I put an optional hyphen in the middle
(*True\~Type*) so it will be hyphenated correctly.

::: calibre9
:::

How do you know whether a word won't be hyphenated correctly? The
problem usually afflicts words that aren't in a standard hyphenation
dictionary, like jargon words, unusual proper names, and other words
with nonstandard spellings, like trade names. As Justice Potter Stewart
might have said, you'll know it when you see it.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to insert an optional hyphen]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Symbol›* ➤ *‹Special Characters›* ➤
> *‹Optional Hyphen›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Symbols›* panel ➤ *‹Symbol›* ➤
> *‹More Symbols›* ➤ *‹Special Characters›* ➤ *‹Optional
> Hyphen›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Line›* ➤ *‹Other Codes›* ➤ *‹Soft
> hyphen›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Not available]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Remember, even though you type a key to insert an optional hyphen, you
won't see it until it's needed.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-811 .mbppagebreak}

::: calibre9
:::

[[math symbols]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Use real symbols, not alphabetic characters]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

If you need *math symbols* infrequently --- some light addition and
subtraction, the occasional negative number --- you can get by without
special symbols. Use the plus sign ( + ) and equals sign ( = ) as usual.
Use the en dash as a minus sign ( -- ) (see [hyphens and
dashes](#calibre_link-20)).

::: calibre9
:::

If you need to do multiplication or beyond, insert real math symbols by
hand.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
123 x 345 - 678 = 41757\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
123 × 345 − 678 = 41757]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

[How to insert math symbols]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Symbol›*. From the *‹Font›* popup
> menu, select *‹Symbol›*. From the character palette that appears,
> double-click the symbol you want.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Symbols›* panel ➤ *‹Symbol›* ➤
> *‹More Symbols›*. From the *‹Font›* popup menu, select *‹Symbol›*.
> From the character palette that appears, double-click the symbol you
> want.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Symbol›*. From the *‹Set›* popup
> menu, select *‹Math / Scientific›*. From the character palette that
> appears, double-click the symbol you want.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Edit›* ➤ *‹Special Characters›*. In the scrolling list
> on the left, go to *‹Symbols›* ➤ *‹Mathematical Symbols›*. From the
> character palette that appears, double-click the symbol you
> want.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The multiplication symbol is also properly part of dimensional
notations like *8.5″× 14″* (not *8.5″x 14″*).

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-833 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-142 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-834 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-835 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-836 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-837 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-838 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-839 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-840 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-841 .mbppagebreak}
[[Type composition](#calibre_link-115) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-115)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[ligatures]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Optional unless the letters f and i collide]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Ligatures* were invented to solve a practical typesetting problem. In
the days of metal fonts, certain characters had features that physically
collided with other characters. To fix this, font makers included
ligatures with their fonts, which combined the troublesome letters into
one piece of type.

::: calibre9
:::

The most common ligatures involve the lowercase f because of its unique
geometry. Other ligatures also exist --- some practical, some
decorative.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000019.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Most digital fonts are designed so that characters don't collide.
Therefore, ligatures don't serve the practical purpose they once did. To
my eye, ligatures bestow a slightly quaint or old-fashioned look on
modern documents.

::: calibre9
:::

If you like that look, great. Use all the ligatures you want.

::: calibre9
:::

I don't like that look. So I turn off ligatures unless there's an actual
collision between the letters f and i. Check this combination in the
bold and italic styles too.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000020.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

The fonts in the first row above have fi combinations that don't
collide. Those fonts will work fine without ligatures. But the fonts in
the second row have fi collisions. Turn on ligatures to correct these
collisions, as seen in the third row.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to turn on ligatures**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003 & 2007** \| Neither supports automatic
> ligatures.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2010** \| Supports ligatures in OpenType fonts only.
> Right-click in the text and select *‹Font›* from the menu. Click the
> *‹Advanced›* tab. Next to *‹Ligatures›*, select *‹Standard Only›* (or
> one of the more elaborate options).]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| Does not support automatic ligatures.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ document icon (first
> icon in the top row) ➤ *‹Document›*. Near the bottom of the panel,
> check *‹Use ligatures›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

"Why are you telling me about ligatures if word-processor support for
them is so limited?" To their fans, ligatures are a hallmark of fine
typesetting. You might turn out to be one of those people. I'm not, but
I don't judge. If you're having a document professionally designed
(e.g., [business cards](#calibre_link-87) or
[letterhead](#calibre_link-86)), you can specify ligatures --- probably
a good idea if you work at *Pfiffner & Piffle LLP*. Word processors are
slowly improving, and in time, ligatures will be an option for more
legal writers. But until then, ligatures are likely to remain on the
typographic sidelines.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Is it possible to insert ligatures manually? Yes. You can either
insert them as you type from a character palette, or you can search and
replace at the end. I don't recommend manually entering ligatures
because they can confuse the spelling checker and the
[hyphenation](#calibre_link-67) engine, and end up causing more problems
than they solve.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-842 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-143 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-843 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-844 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-845 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-846 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-847 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-848 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-849 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-850 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-851 .mbppagebreak}
[[Text formatting]{.bold1}]{.calibre21}

::: calibre3
:::

When I tell people I write about typography, they often say, "Oh, you
mean fonts?"

::: calibre9
:::

Yes and no. Sure, fonts are an important part of typography. But there's
much more to typography than fonts.

::: calibre9
:::

Similarly, there's more to text formatting than what font to use. Text
formatting includes everything that affects the appearance of the
characters on the page --- not only fonts, but also point size, bold and
italic styles, small caps, letterspacing, and kerning.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000021.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[[Above: []{#calibre_link-269}Specimen of fonts offered by William
Caslon, 1728.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The first part of this chapter covers basic and advanced formatting
rules. The second part is a gallery of font samples --- for those ready
to look beyond the limitations of [system fonts](#calibre_link-43).

::: calibre9
:::

In this chapter, there aren't as many bright lines between correct and
incorrect habits as in the last chapter. You won't be able to get by on
rote application of rules. You'll need to start making typographic
judgments of your own.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-852 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-144 .calibre}

::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-854 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-855 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::: {#calibre_link-856 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::: {#calibre_link-857 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::: {#calibre_link-858 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::: {#calibre_link-859 .mbppagebreak}
::::::: {#calibre_link-860 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-865 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-866 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-867 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-868 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-869 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-870 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-871 .mbppagebreak}

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[underlining]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Absolutely not]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

In a printed document, don't underline. Ever. It's ugly and it makes
text harder to read. See for yourself ---

::: calibre9
:::

> [Underlining is another holdover from the typewriter age. Typewriters
> had no bold or italic styling. So the only way to emphasize text was
> to back up the carriage and type underscores beneath the text. It was
> a workaround for shortcomings in typewriter technology.]{.underline}

::: calibre9
:::

> Underlining is another holdover from the typewriter age. Typewriters
> had no bold or italic styling. So the only way to emphasize text was
> to back up the carriage and type underscores beneath the text. It was
> a workaround for shortcomings in typewriter technology.

::: calibre9
:::

Your word processor does not suffer from these shortcomings. If you feel
the urge to underline, use [bold or italic](#calibre_link-44) instead.
In special situations, like [headings](#calibre_link-47), you can also
consider using [all caps](#calibre_link-45), [small
caps](#calibre_link-53), or a change in [point size](#calibre_link-46).

::: calibre9
:::

Not convinced? I invite you to find a book, newspaper, or magazine that
underlines text. I notice it only in the tabloids. Is that the look
you're going for? No, I didn't think so.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Another reason underlining looks worse than bold or italic:
underlining is mechanically applied by the word processor. Bold and
italic styles are specially designed to match the basic style of the
font.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The "track changes" feature of your word processor will underline text
added to the document. This is fine. In fact, it's one more reason *not*
to use underlining for emphasis --- to avoid confusing text marked as a
revision with text that happens to be underlined.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ "But the *Bluebook* requires underlining." No, it doesn't. In its
rules for practitioners, the *Bluebook* chooses to "keep\[ \] with the
tradition of underlining certain text," but practitioners "may
substitute italic type wherever underlining is used." *Bluebook* at 3
(19th ed. 2010). Subsequent *Bluebook* rules say to "underscore (or
italicize)." (For instance, *Bluebook* rule B1.) So unless you're using
a typewriter, fulfill your *Bluebook* destiny by italicizing, not
underlining.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ On a website, it's idiomatic to underline clickable text (also known
as *hyperlinks*). But don't underline other text --- visitors will be
confused when their clicking goes unanswered.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-875 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[goofy fonts]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Don't use them]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

I once met a lawyer who had actually set his letterhead in a font called
Stencil:

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000022.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

What was his target clientele? Army-surplus stores? He explained that he
wanted something distinctive.

::: calibre9
:::

Distinctive is fine. Goofy is not.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000023.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

From the top: no, no, no, no, and hell no.

::: calibre9
:::

Novelty fonts, script fonts, handwriting fonts, circus fonts --- these
have no place in any document created by a lawyer. Save it for your next
career as a designer of breakfast-cereal boxes.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-888 .mbppagebreak}

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[monospaced fonts]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Don't use these either]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The [system fonts](#calibre_link-43) Courier, Monaco, and Consolas are
examples of *monospaced fonts*, so named because every character is the
same width. Most other fonts are proportionally spaced, meaning the
characters vary in width.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000024.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

The samples above are set at the same [point size](#calibre_link-46).
But the monospaced font (first and third rows) takes up more horizontal
space than the proportional font (second and fourth rows). The
differences are most noticeable in characters that are narrow in the
proportional font, like f, i, j, l, r, t, and the punctuation
characters.

::: calibre9
:::

Monospaced fonts were invented to meet the mechanical requirements of
typewriters. They were not invented to win beauty contests. Compared to
proportional fonts, monospaced fonts are harder to read. And because
they take up more horizontal space, you'll always get fewer words per
page with a monospaced font.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[In 1944, IBM introduced a typewriter that could handle proportional
> fonts. But the technology never took off.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

There are no good reasons to use monospaced fonts. So don't. Use
proportional fonts.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If you practice in one of the few courts that require a monospaced
font, you can still do better than Courier. See [Font
samples](#calibre_link-57) for better options, including FB Alix, a font
family I designed to show that monospaced fonts for [body
text](#calibre_link-66) don't have to be tedious.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Do you need monospaced numerals --- typographers call them *tabular
figures* --- so that columns of numbers will line up? This is such a
common need that most proportional fonts include tabular figures by
default. To check if your font has them, type a line of zeroes above a
line of ones. If they're the same length, the font has tabular figures.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000025.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Do you need to quote software code or HTML in your document? Then use
a monospaced font for one of the reasons software engineers do ---
software code includes compressed syntax like (int i=1; i\<111; i++)
which is more legible when set in a monospaced font
`(int i=1; i<111; i++)`{.calibre28}.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-902 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-149 .calibre}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-904 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-905 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-906 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-907 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-908 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-909 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-910 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-911 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-912 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-913 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-914 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-915 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-916 .mbppagebreak}
[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[system fonts]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Choose wisely]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*System fonts* are the fonts already installed on your computer. Some
are better than others. In printed documents they present three
problems.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  *Many system fonts are not very good*. This is less of a problem on
    the Mac. But some of the Windows system fonts are among the most
    awful on the planet. I won't name names, but my least favorite
    rhymes with Barial.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  *Many system fonts have been optimized for screen legibility, not
    print*. This legibility comes at the cost of design details, which
    have been sanded off because they don't reproduce well on screen
    (e.g., Georgia, Verdana, Cambria, and Calibri). Screen-optimized
    fonts look clunky on the printed page.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    ![](images/000026.jpg){.calibre22}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    Compare the two fonts above. In basic appearance, they're similar.
    But []{#calibre_link-181}Georgia was optimized for the screen;
    Miller was optimized for print. See the difference?

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[In one square inch, an LCD screen that displays 100 dots per
    > inch has less than 3% of the resolution of a laser printer with
    > 600 dots per inch, so rendering a font accurately is much more
    > difficult. It's analogous to the problem of scanning a paper
    > document accurately --- lower resolution means lower
    > fidelity.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  *All system fonts are overexposed*. Because these fonts are included
    with millions of computers, they're used all the time. Not every
    typography project demands novelty, but if yours does, you'll need
    to look elsewhere. For instance, you wouldn't want to adopt the
    marketing slogan "A Law Firm Unlike Any Other" and then set it in
    Helvetica.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

If you're limited to system fonts, consult this chart and choose wisely.
For print, fonts on the A list are always best. For screen display, like
presentations and websites, system fonts on the A, B, and C lists are
fine. They're also suitable for sharing draft documents. But always
steer clear of the F list.

::: calibre9
:::

Fonts plausible for [body text](#calibre_link-66) are marked with ★.
Others are usable for special purposes (for instance,
[letterhead](#calibre_link-86)).

::: calibre9
:::

This chart includes all the common Windows and Mac system fonts, plus
the Microsoft Office fonts. System configurations differ, so not every
font will be on your computer. If you don't see it in your font menu,
it's not installed.

::: calibre9
:::

These rankings represent a blend of practical and aesthetic
considerations, not absolute merit. Some fonts on the F list aren't bad,
they're just inapt for a law office. Similarly, some fonts on the A list
are not my favorites, but they're reasonably useful.

::: calibre9
:::

**WARNING**

::: calibre9
:::

This chart is offered only as a harm-reduction device. In the long term,
a diet of system fonts can be harmful to your health. My official advice
remains the same: lawyers are professional writers, and professional
writers should use professional fonts. In [Font
samples](#calibre_link-57), I suggest professional fonts that are good
alternatives to the most common system fonts.

::: calibre9
:::

**The A list: Generally tolerable**

::: calibre9
:::

- Baskerville (Mac) ★

- Book Antiqua ★

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ "If my PDF will probably be read on screen, shouldn't I use a
screen-optimized system font?" No. In Windows, fonts are optimized for
the screen using *hinting*, which is extra software code stored in the
font itself. Windows relies on this hinting when it draws text on screen
(e.g., in Microsoft Word). But Adobe Acrobat --- what most people use to
read PDFs --- draws text on screen using its own technology that ignores
the hinting. So in PDF, system fonts lose their screen-legibility
advantage over other fonts. Any PDF could also end up being printed.
Therefore, as a rule, you're better off using print-optimized fonts for
PDFs, regardless of how you expect the PDF to be read.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Adobe uses its own text-rendering technology in Acrobat so that PDF
> documents display the same way on screen regardless of the underlying
> operating system.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ "But if I use a print-optimized professional font in my PDF instead of
a system font, my readers probably won't have the same font installed."
Right. But it doesn't matter. When you generate a PDF, your fonts are
embedded in the PDF to preserve the formatting.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ My aversion to Comic Sans --- king of the [goofy
fonts](#calibre_link-41) --- probably comes as no surprise. But why
[]{#calibre_link-175}Arial? Arial was created as a Helvetica substitute.
To many, they're indistinguishable.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000027.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

But to typographers, Arial contains none of the consistency and balance
that makes Helvetica successful. For instance, the ends of the lowercase
a, c, e, g, s, and t in Helvetica are exactly horizontal. In Arial,
those ends are sloped arbitrarily. Reading Arial is like trying to have
dinner on a tippy restaurant table.

::: calibre9
:::

As a system font, Arial has achieved ubiquity akin to [Times New
Roman](#calibre_link-108). And like Times New Roman, Arial is
permanently associated with the work of people who will never care about
typography.

::: calibre9
:::

You're not one of those people. So use [Helvetica](#calibre_link-150).
Use [Franklin Gothic](#calibre_link-151). Use [Gill
Sans](#calibre_link-152). Use one of the other substitutes for Arial in
[Font samples](#calibre_link-57). But don't use Arial.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-917 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-153 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-918 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-919 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-920 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-921 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-922 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-923 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-924 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-925 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-926 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-927 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-928 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-929 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-930 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-931 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-932 .mbppagebreak}
[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[bold or italic]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[One or the other, as little as possible]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Bold *or* italic --- always think of them as mutually exclusive. That is
the first rule.

::: calibre9
:::

The second rule is to use bold and italic as little as possible. They
are tools for emphasis. But if everything is emphasized, then nothing is
emphasized. Also, because bold and italic styles are designed to
contrast with regular roman text, they're somewhat harder to read.
They're fine for short pieces of text, but not for long stretches.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Text that is neither bold nor italic is called
> **roman**.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

**Nevertheless, some lawyers --- let's call them *overemphasizers* ---
just can't get enough bold and italic. If they feel strongly about the
point they're making, they won't hesitate to run the whole paragraph in
bold type. Don't be one of these people. This habit wears down your
readers' retinas and their patience. It also gives you nowhere to go
when you need to emphasize a word. That's no problem for
overemphasizers, who resort to [underlining bold text]{.underline} or
*using bold italic. [These are both bad ideas.]{.underline}***

::: calibre9
:::

With a serif font, use italic for gentle emphasis, or bold for heavier
emphasis.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000028.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

If you're using a sans serif font, skip italic and use bold for
emphasis. It's usually not worth italicizing sans serif fonts --- unlike
serif fonts, which look quite different when italicized, most sans serif
italic fonts just have a gentle slant that doesn't stand out on the
page.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000029.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[**Serif fonts** like Georgia and Palatino have "feet" protruding
> from the ends of vertical strokes. These feet are the serifs. Serif
> rhymes with **sheriff**, not **sir reef**.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[**Sans serif fonts** like Helvetica and Verdana do not have these
> feet. Though they are associated with contemporary typography, sans
> serif fonts date from the 1810s. Sans rhymes with **hands**, not
> **cons**. Avoid the common misspelling **san
> serif**.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Foreign words used in English are sometimes italicized, sometimes not,
depending on how common they are. For instance, you would italicize your
*bête noire* and your *Weltanschauung*, but not your croissant or your
résumé. When in doubt, consult a dictionary or usage guide.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ One reason I don't recommend sans serif fonts for [body
text](#calibre_link-66) is because they have weak italic styles. Legal
citations are more legible with a distinct italic.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ See [headings](#calibre_link-47) for tips on how to avoid escalating
overemphasis when working with multiple heading levels.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If you need another option for emphasis, consider [small
caps](#calibre_link-53).

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Some fonts have both a bold style and a semibold style. You can use
either for emphasis. I usually prefer bold to semibold because I like
the greater contrast. But semibold is a little easier to read.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Some fonts have styles that are heavier than bold, like black or
ultra. These weights are usually intended for large sizes (for instance,
headlines) and don't work well at the 10- to 12-point range of most
[body text](#calibre_link-66).

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-933 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-154 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-934 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-935 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-936 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-937 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-938 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-939 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-940 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-941 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-942 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-943 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-944 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-945 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-946 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-947 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-948 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-949 .mbppagebreak}
[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[all caps]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Fine for less than one line]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*All-caps text* --- meaning text with all the letters capitalized --- is
best used sparingly.

::: calibre9
:::

At standard [body text](#calibre_link-66) sizes, capital letters --- or
simply *caps* --- are harder to read than normal lowercase text. Why? We
read more lowercase text, so as a matter of habit, lowercase is more
familiar and thus more legible. Furthermore, cognitive research has
suggested that the shapes of lowercase letters --- some tall (dhkl),
some short (aens), some descending (gypq) --- create a varied visual
contour that helps our brain recognize words. Capitalization homogenizes
these shapes, leaving a rectangular contour.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[[VARIED:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
> Legible Shapes\
> [[RECTANGULAR:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
> LEGIBLE SHAPES]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

That doesn't mean you shouldn't use caps. Just use them judiciously.
Caps are suitable for headings shorter than one line (e.g., "Table of
Authorities"), headers, footers, captions, or other labels. Caps work at
small [point sizes](#calibre_link-46). Caps work well on
[letterhead](#calibre_link-86) and [business cards](#calibre_link-87).
Always add [letterspacing](#calibre_link-49) to caps to make them easier
to read, and make sure [kerning](#calibre_link-48) is turned on.

::: calibre9
:::

DON'T CAPITALIZE WHOLE PARAGRAPHS. THIS HABIT IS ENDEMIC TO LAWYERS, BUT
IT'S ESPECIALLY COMMON IN CONTRACTS. MANY LAWYERS SEEM TO THINK THAT
CAPITALIZATION COMMUNICATES AUTHORITY AND IMPORTANCE. "HEY, LOOK HERE,
I'M A LAWYER! I DEMAND THAT YOU PAY ATTENTION TO THIS!" BUT A PARAGRAPH
SET IN ALL CAPS IS VERY HARD TO READ. **AND IT'S EVEN HARDER TO READ IF
IT'S BOLD. AS THE PARAGRAPH WEARS ON, READERS FATIGUE. INTEREST WANES.
HOW ABOUT YOU? DO YOU ENJOY READING THIS? I DOUBT IT. BUT I REGULARLY
SEE CAPITALIZED PARAGRAPHS IN LEGAL DOCUMENTS THAT ARE MUCH LONGER THAN
THIS. DO YOUR READERS A FAVOR. STOP CAPITALIZING WHOLE PARAGRAPHS.**

::: calibre9
:::

All-caps paragraphs are an example of self-defeating typography. If you
need readers to pay attention to an important part of your document, the
last thing you want is for them to skim over it. But that's what
inevitably happens with all-caps paragraphs, because they're so
difficult to read.

::: calibre9
:::

To emphasize a paragraph, you have better options. Use [rules and
borders](#calibre_link-71). Add a [heading](#calibre_link-47) that
labels it **Important**. Run it in a larger [point
size](#calibre_link-46). But don't capitalize it.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[The terms **uppercase** and **lowercase** come from traditional
> print shops. Capital letters, used less frequently, were stored in a
> case on a shelf above the other letters (then called **minuscule**
> letters).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

There are two ways to put caps in a document. The popular method is to
engage the caps-lock key at the left edge of the keyboard and type away.
That works, but it makes capitalization a permanent feature of your
text.

::: calibre9
:::

The preferred method is to apply all-caps formatting to normally typed
text. That way, you can toggle capitalization on and off without
retyping the text itself.

::: calibre9
:::

+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+
|                 | [[Word]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[WordPerfect]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} | [[Pages]{.bold1}]{.calibre7} |
|                 |                             |                                    |                              |
|                 | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | :::                          |
+=================+=============================+====================================+==============================+
| all caps        | control +\                  | control + k\                       | *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Font›*\       |
|                 | shift + a                   | (converts case)                    | ➤ *‹Capitalization›*\        |
| ::: calibre9    |                             |                                    | ➤ *‹All Caps›*               |
| :::             | ::: calibre9                | ::: calibre9                       |                              |
|                 | :::                         | :::                                | ::: calibre9                 |
|                 |                             |                                    | :::                          |
+-----------------+-----------------------------+------------------------------------+------------------------------+

::: calibre17
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ It can be useful to capitalize specially defined terms to distinguish
them from their generic counterparts. But capitalize only the first
letter. Don't set the whole word in caps, or worse, bold caps. Likewise,
you can refer to party Omicron Motor Company as Omicron --- you don't
need to write its name OMICRON or **OMICRON**. When you set a word like
**OMICRON** in caps, you're putting a visual speed bump in every
sentence that mentions **OMICRON**. As the habit multiplies, soon you're
talking about how **OMICRON** and **SIGMA** conspired to make
**AIRBAGS** that injured the **PLAINTIFF** and the rest of the
**CLASS**. At that point, you haven't made your **DOCUMENT** easier to
**READ**. You've only **MADE** it more **ANNOYING**.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Sometimes caps are required by law --- for instance, California
requires that defined terms in discovery requests be set in all caps.
(E.g., Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2030.060(e).) And sometimes caps are
prohibited by law --- for instance, New York court rules say that
"\[e\]xcept in headings, words shall not be in bold type or type
consisting of all capital letters." (22 N.Y.C.R.R. 500.1(j).)

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Don't assume caps are either necessary or sufficient to satisfy a law
requiring "conspicuous" text. For instance, the Uniform Commercial Code
defines "conspicuous" as "written, displayed, or presented \[so\] that a
reasonable person against which it is to operate ought to have noticed
it," but notes that "\[w\]hether a term is 'conspicuous' or not is a
decision for the court." (UCC § 1-201(b)(10).) In California, an appeals
court held that an insurance disclaimer was not automatically
"conspicuous" under the UCC even though it was printed in caps. (See
*Broberg v. Guardian Life Ins. Co. of America*, 171 Cal. App. 4th 912,
922 (2009).)

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If your all-caps text contains a citation with a small-letter
subdivision, don't capitalize the subdivision letter --- it may render
the citation ambiguous or incorrect.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
CIV. CODE § 1744(A)\
[[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
CIV. CODE § 1744(a)]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ "Why reject [underlining](#calibre_link-40) but not caps? Aren't they
both typewriter habits?" No. Caps are the original alphabetic
characters. They are part of the oldest traditions of our written
language; underlining cannot claim a similar pedigree. Caps in English
descend directly from the Latin alphabet. (That's why basic, unstyled
fonts are called *roman.*) Through the early Middle Ages, scribes in
Europe adapted the Latin alphabet into smaller, more casual forms,
called *minuscules*. In the 700s, Charlemagne started a project to
create a standardized script across his empire. That script,
*Carolingian minuscule*, spread through Europe and popularized the
combination of uppercase and lowercase letters that's been a feature of
printed European languages since then.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ To those lawyers who type e-mails in all caps: enough already. YOU
DON'T HAVE TO SHOUT. WE CAN HEAR YOU JUST FINE.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-950 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-155 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-951 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-952 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-953 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-954 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-955 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-956 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-957 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-958 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-959 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-960 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-961 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-962 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-963 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-964 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-965 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-966 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-967 .mbppagebreak}
[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[point size]{.bold1}]{.calibre20}

[[Smaller on paper; bigger on screen]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The *point size* of your text can be smaller than you think. The optimal
point size for [body text](#calibre_link-66) in printed documents is
between 10 and 12 point.

::: calibre9
:::

While courts often require text to be set at 12 point --- and sometimes
larger --- it's not the most comfortable size for reading. If you
compare a court filing with the average book, newspaper, or magazine,
you'll notice that the text in the filing is larger.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000030.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[There are 72 **points** to an inch. Word lets you specify point
> sizes in half-point increments. WordPerfect and Pages allow finer
> increments of one-tenth of a point.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

It's very difficult to find a professionally designed book, newspaper,
or magazine with 12-point body text. One major reason is cost --- bigger
point sizes require more paper.

::: calibre9
:::

But I can't guarantee 12 point will always look too big. That's because
the point-size system is not absolute --- different fonts set at the
same point size won't necessarily appear the same on the page.

::: calibre9
:::

That means you need to let your eyes be the judge. Don't just rely on
the point size. For instance, the three fonts below --- Sabon, [Times
New Roman](#calibre_link-108), and Arno --- are set at 12 point, but
they're not the same size visually.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000031.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

You can match the length of two fonts by setting a block of text twice:
once in the old font and once in the new font, both at the same point
size. Adjust the point size of the new font until each line of text
breaks in roughly the same place. (You won't be able to match them
exactly.) Below, the point sizes of Sabon and Arno have been adjusted so
they occupy the same space as Times New Roman.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000032.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

The point size can be even smaller in professionally typeset materials
like publications and stationery. Text on [business
cards](#calibre_link-87) is often only seven or eight points.
[All-caps](#calibre_link-45) text is often just as legible as regular
lowercase text at these sizes.

::: calibre9
:::

For websites, I recommend 12 point or larger for body text. On screen,
even 13 or 14 point can be a comfortable reading size. The web has a
tradition of teeny fonts --- web designers have long insisted on
accommodating some hypothetical guy in Sioux Falls who's still using a
Windows 95 machine, a 13-inch monitor, and a 2400-baud modem. I feel
comfortable ignoring him.

::: calibre9
:::

[Point size as emphasis]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

It's fine to emphasize text with a larger point size (or de-emphasize it
with a smaller point size). If you use [bold or
italic](#calibre_link-44), or [all caps](#calibre_link-45), the styling
is binary: it's either **ON** or off. But point size offers a subtle
range of adjustments.

::: calibre9
:::

The key word: *subtle*. If the text is set at 11 point, you don't need
to go up to 14 point for emphasis. Start with a small increase --- say,
half a point --- and move up in half-point increments until you get the
emphasis you need.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[You can also emphasize text by applying bold or all-caps formatting,
> and then reducing the point size slightly. Sounds strange but it can
> work.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ In a legal document, I can't imagine any reason to use a font smaller
than seven point or larger than 30 point.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ In [hyphens and dashes](#calibre_link-20), I mentioned that *em*
refers to a typographer's measurement, not the letter M. The em size of
a font is the same as its point size. Digital fonts are made of scalable
outlines, not metal, but the em terminology persists. Digital fonts are
drawn inside a rectangle called the em. To render a font on screen, your
computer scales the em to match the current point size. Two fonts set at
the same point size will appear to be different sizes if one occupies
less space on its em.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ As you reduce point size, also reduce [line spacing](#calibre_link-63)
and [line length](#calibre_link-64). For instance, newspaper fonts are
quite small, but remain legible because they have snug line spacing and
short line length.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Most courts control the length of briefs with limits on point size and
page length. In the typewriter age, this worked because typewriter
output was standardized. In the digital age, it makes less sense, since
artful formatting and layout can make documents appear longer or shorter
as necessary. (If you're unclear on the concept, ask someone who's
written a college paper in the last 20 years.) Courts, law professors,
and anyone else who needs to set standards for document length would be
better off putting these rules in terms of word count. Unlike
typewriters, all word processors have a word-count function. Compared to
page limits, word counts are harder to evade. To be fair, they're also
harder to verify.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Can you determine the point size of a font by measuring it? No.
Because of the differences in apparent sizing between fonts, there's
nothing you can measure that would be conclusive. The only way to figure
it out is to set the same text, in the same font, with the same [line
length](#calibre_link-64). Then adjust the point size so it matches the
printed sample.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ [If lawyers have established a reputation for anything typographic, it
is our reputed affection for *fine print*. Fine print is synonymous with
evasion and deception. I have some trepidation about advising that "the
point size of your text can be smaller than you think" because I don't
want to encourage the fine-print abusers out there. You know who you
are. ("Did you hear that? Butterick said we can crank it down *even
smaller*!")]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[Good typography reinforces the goals of the text (as you may remember
from [what is good typography](#calibre_link-11)). Lawyers are
advocates, so when I dispense typographic advice, I'm careful not to
take a position on the propriety of certain habits.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[For instance, as a consumer, I don't like getting a credit-card
contract that's an acre of six-point type. But if I were a lawyer for
the credit-card company, my job would be to advance the interests of my
client, including the typography.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[If that seems cruelly pragmatic, consider direct-mail solicitations. I
find them ugly and unappealing. But I also know their design is the
result of years of testing and refinement about how to get people to
part with their money. Is that good typography? In context,
yes.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

["Did you hear that? Butterick said that fine print can be *good
typography*!" Touché, I guess.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-988 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-989 .mbppagebreak}

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Advanced
