::: calibre9
:::

[headings]{.bold1}

[[Fewer levels, subtler emphasis]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Headings* present two sets of problems for lawyers: structural and
typographic. Cure the structural problems and the typographic problems
become much simpler.

::: calibre9
:::

The first structural problem is that lawyers often use too many levels
of headings. This leads to increasingly desperate attempts to make them
visually distinct, usually with injudicious combinations of [bold or
italic](#calibre_link-44), [underlining](#calibre_link-40), [point
size](#calibre_link-46), [all caps](#calibre_link-45), and [first-line
indents](#calibre_link-61). The result is pileups like this:

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000033.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Headings are signposts for readers that reveal the structure of your
argument. Note that I didn't say the structure of your *document*.
Headings that announce every topic, subtopic, minitopic, and microtopic
are exhausting. If you write from an outline, that can be a good
starting point for your headings, but don't stop there --- simplify it
further.

::: calibre9
:::

Limit yourself to three levels of headings. Two is better. Readers
should be able to orient themselves from the headings. With more than
three levels, that task becomes hopelessly confusing. You may know your
argument inside out, but no one else does.

::: calibre9
:::

The second structural problem is headings that are just placeholders or
labels. Write substantive headings. Readers should be able to understand
the framework of your argument just by reading the headings. Headings
without substance are not signposts --- they just waste space. Here's a
bad heading:

::: calibre9
:::

> **I. Introduction**

::: calibre9
:::

Whatever appears under the first heading is obviously the introduction.
So avoid a generic label and make the heading descriptive. Is your
introduction framing the legal issue? Summarizing the procedural
history? Reciting facts? Then say so:

::: calibre9
:::

> **I. Legal issue**

::: calibre9
:::

> **I. Procedural history**

::: calibre9
:::

> **I. Facts**

::: calibre9
:::

Another bad heading:

::: calibre9
:::

> **B. For the reasons below, the Court should find that it has personal
> jurisdiction over the defendant.**

::: calibre9
:::

This heading isn't a signpost --- it's a roadblock. Headings preview
what's ahead. So move the argument forward. An improvement:

::: calibre9
:::

> **B. The Court has personal jurisdiction over MegaCorp because its
> headquarters are in San Luis Obispo.**

::: calibre9
:::

> [[In journalism, **burying the lead** describes the error of putting
> the key idea of your story somewhere in the middle. Traditionally it
> was spelled **lede**, not **lead**, to distinguish it from the
> material used for casting metal type.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Once you've cured the structural problems, the typographic problems are
easy to solve. If you simplify your headings, you'll end up needing only
two or three levels. Then work within these parameters:

::: calibre9
:::

1.  Don't use [all caps](#calibre_link-45). If your headings are full
    sentences, they're too long for caps.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Don't underline. Why? Review [underlining](#calibre_link-40).

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Don't center, subject to the exceptions in [centered
    text](#calibre_link-59).

    ::: calibre9
    :::

4.  Use bold, not italic. For headings, bold is easier to read than
    italic and stands out better on the page. And since the choice is
    [bold or italic](#calibre_link-44) --- not both --- you should
    prefer bold. (But it's still an option, not a requirement. Non-bold
    headings work too.)

    ::: calibre9
    :::

5.  It's fine to make the [point size](#calibre_link-46) bigger, but
    just a little. Use the smallest increment necessary to make a
    visible difference. If your text is set in 12 point, you needn't go
    up to 14 or 15 point. Try a smaller increase --- to 12.5 or 13
    point.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[If you're using bold in your heading, you can also try reducing
    > the point size by a half or full point. If your font has a
    > relatively heavy bold style (like [Times New
    > Roman](#calibre_link-108)), reducing the size slightly can offset
    > the effect of the darker color, giving you subtler
    > emphasis.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

6.  Only use two levels of indenting, even if you use more than two
    levels of headings. Some lawyers like to indent every heading a
    little farther. Bad idea. Once your heading is no longer anchored to
    something on the left edge of the text, it's just floating in space.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

7.  Suppress [hyphenation](#calibre_link-67) in headings.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

8.  Use the [keep lines together](#calibre_link-73) and [keep with next
    paragraph](#calibre_link-74) options to prevent headings from
    breaking awkwardly across pages.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

See [hierarchical headings](#calibre_link-54) for tips on how to number
headings.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1007 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Advanced

::: calibre9
:::

[kerning]{.bold1}

[[Turn it on]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Kerning* is the adjustment of specific pairs of letters to improve
spacing and fit. (It differs from [letterspacing](#calibre_link-49),
which affects all pairs.) Most fonts come with hundreds and sometimes
thousands of kerning pairs inserted by the font designer.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[It's also possible to manually kern letter pairs. For professional
> typographers, this is a mandatory skill, but for everyone else, the
> built-in kerning is adequate.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Below, notice how kerning reduces the large gaps between certain letter
pairs, making them consistent with the rest of the font.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000034.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Always use kerning. By default, kerning is not activated in Word or
WordPerfect, so you have to turn it on yourself. If you use [paragraph
and character styles](#calibre_link-80), turn on kerning as part of your
style definitions.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to turn on kerning**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Font›* from the
> menu. Click the *‹Character Spacing›* tab. (In Word 2010 it's called
> the *‹Advanced›* tab.) Check the box that says *‹Kerning for fonts
> \_\_\_\_ Points and above›*. Put the number 8 in the point-size
> box.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Typesetting›* ➤ *‹Word/Letter
> Spacing›* ➤ *‹Automatic Kerning›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Kerning is on by default and cannot be turned
> off.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1028 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1029 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1030 .mbppagebreak}

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[letterspacing]{.bold1}

[[Use 5--12% extra space with caps, but not with
lowercase]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Letterspacing* (also known as *character spacing* or *tracking*) is the
adjustment of the horizontal white space between the letters in a block
of text. Unlike [kerning](#calibre_link-48), which affects only
designated pairs of letters, letterspacing affects every pair.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000035.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Lowercase letters don't ordinarily need letterspacing. Capital letters
usually appear at the beginning of a word or sentence, so they're
designed to fit correctly next to lowercase letters. But when you use
capital letters together, that spacing is too tight.

::: calibre9
:::

That's why you always add 5--12% extra letterspacing to text in [all
caps](#calibre_link-45) or [small caps](#calibre_link-53). This is
particularly important at small sizes (e.g., the footer of a court
filing).

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to set letterspacing**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Font›* from the
> menu. Click the *‹Character Spacing›* tab. (In Word 2010 it's called
> the *‹Advanced›* tab.) On the line that says *‹Spacing›*, in the box
> on the right, enter the amount of letterspacing. Letterspacing in Word
> is measured in points. Use 0.6--1.4 points of letterspacing for every
> 12 points of point size (this corresponds to 5--12%).]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Typesetting›* ➤ *‹Word/Letter
> Spacing›*. In the box labeled *‹Letterspacing: Percent of Optimal›*,
> enter the amount of letterspacing. Letterspacing in WordPerfect is
> measured as a percentage of normal spacing. Use 105--112%.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left). Use the *‹Character›* slider to add letterspacing.
> Letterspacing in Pages is measured as percent added. Use
> 5--12%.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

These are not absolute limits --- use your judgment. But avoid the
common error of spreading letters too far apart. If the spaces between
letters are large enough to fit more letters, you've gone overboard.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000036.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Typographer Frederic Goudy is famously credited with opining that
"Anyone who would letterspace lowercase would steal sheep." But a few
sources claim that his original comment concerned blackletter fonts, not
lowercase, and that he used a more colorful verb than "steal."

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000037.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ I accept the minority view on Goudy's comment because, as Goudy was
doubtless aware, sometimes lowercase should be letterspaced. Fonts
intended for [body text](#calibre_link-66) have spacing optimized for
body-text point sizes (approximately 9--13 point). But typographers will
often add letterspacing to lowercase text smaller than nine point in
order to keep the spaces between letters distinct. Similarly,
typographers will often remove letterspacing from lowercase text used at
larger sizes (e.g., headlines).

::: calibre9
:::

➤ As with [kerning](#calibre_link-48), if you use [paragraph and
character styles](#calibre_link-80) to make a style with all caps or
small caps, include letterspacing as part of the style definition.

::: calibre9
:::

::: calibre9
:::

[color]{.bold1}

[[Black is best]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Color* used to be irrelevant to a law office that only had access to
monochrome printers and copiers. These days, color output devices are
cheap and ubiquitous, so the topic deserves a brief mention.

::: calibre9
:::

Printed documents intended for reading (e.g.,
[motions](#calibre_link-84), [research memos](#calibre_link-85),
letters) should always be set in black type. No exceptions.

::: calibre9
:::

Some documents (e.g., [letterhead](#calibre_link-86), [business
cards](#calibre_link-87)) can include text set in color, but use it
judiciously. Multiple shades of one color are usually better than
multiple contrasting colors.

::: calibre9
:::

In printed documents, avoid white text on a dark background, known as
*knockout* text. Knockout text uses about 20 times more toner than
regular text, making it a very expensive effect on an office laser
printer.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ On websites, consider making your text dark gray rather than black.
Unlike a piece of paper --- which reflects ambient light --- a
computer's LCD screen projects its own light and tends to have more
severe contrast. Therefore, on screen, dark gray text is often more
comfortable to read than black text. That's why many digital-book
readers let you reduce the screen brightness or change the text color.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ I recommend color for digital [Bates numbering](#calibre_link-79)
because it's meant to stand out on the page.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ I don't like to use gray backgrounds in documents intended for laser
printing. The original might look fine, but subsequent copying or
scanning tends to make the gray backgrounds darker and grittier, making
the text less legible.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1073 .mbppagebreak}

[ordinals]{.bold1}

[[No superscripts]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Ordinals* are numbers that express position in a series. By default,
your word processor will convert ordinals into superscripts.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[SUPERSCRIPTS:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
1^st^ 2^nd^ 304^th^\
[[NO SUPERSCRIPTS:]{.bold1}]{.calibre26}\
1st 2nd 304th]{.calibre25}

::: calibre9
:::

Superscripted ordinals have two problems. First, ordinals turn up most
often in citations. The *Bluebook* and other citation authorities forbid
superscripted ordinals (e.g., *Bluebook* rule 6.2(b)(i) (19th ed.
2010)). Second, superscripted ordinals are tiny and hard to read.

::: calibre9
:::

The best practice is to avoid superscripted ordinals. To do this, you
have to adjust your word processor's default behavior.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to turn off superscripted ordinals**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹AutoCorrect Options›* ➤ *‹AutoFormat
> As You Type›* ➤ uncheck *‹Ordinals (1st) with
> superscript›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007** \| Office menu ➤ *‹Word Options›* ➤ *‹Proofing›* ➤
> *‹AutoCorrect Options›* ➤ *‹AutoFormat As You Type›* ➤ uncheck
> *‹Ordinals (1st) with superscript›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2010** \| *‹File›* ➤ *‹Options›* ➤ *‹Proofing›* ➤
> *‹AutoCorrect Options›* ➤ *‹AutoFormat As You Type›* ➤ uncheck
> *‹Ordinals (1st) with superscript›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹QuickCorrect›* ➤
> *‹Format-As-You-Go›* ➤ uncheck *‹QuickOrdinals›* (under
> *‹Format-As-You-Go Choices›*)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Pages›* ➤ *‹Preferences›* ➤ *‹Auto-Correction›* ➤
> uncheck *‹Superscript numerical suffixes›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Advanced

::: calibre9
:::

[web and e-mail addresses]{.bold1}

[[Don't hyphenate]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Web addresses* identify a location on the Internet. They usually take
the form http://www.somelongname.com/folder/folder/page.html. *E-mail
addresses* usually take the form nameofperson@somelongname.com.

::: calibre9
:::

Web addresses present two problems.

::: calibre9
:::

The first problem: web addresses can be long. Really, really long.
Running the whole web address may be fine if you're writing a law-review
footnote and just need to show where you got your material. But it's
useless if you're hoping readers will type the address on their own.

::: calibre9
:::

For a more usable web address, use an address-shortening service like
TinyURL or Bit.ly. These services take a web address of any length and
convert it into a short address like <http://tinyurl.com/p5wf3c>. This
is easier to read and type. But it doesn't reveal the underlying web
address. It also isn't guaranteed to work permanently.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[**URL** stands for **Uniform Resource Locator**. It's another name
> for a web address.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

If you put a web address in a citation, consider running the long
version with a shortened version next to it. Then you're covered. For
instance:

::: calibre9
:::

> See
> http://www.cacd.uscourts.gov/CACD/LocRules.nsf/a224d2a6f8771599882567cc005e9d79/043e35b5803a6d3f8825768d0067b375?OpenDocument,
> also available at <http://tinyurl.com/y6o4yte>.

::: calibre9
:::

The second problem: web addresses are difficult to wrap onto multiple
lines. A web address is one unbroken string of characters. You don't
want your web address hyphenated, because readers will likely mistake
the hyphens for part of the address. Therefore, use [hard line
breaks](#calibre_link-32) to set the points where the web address should
wrap onto the next line.

::: calibre9
:::

E-mail addresses are shorter than web addresses and aren't as painful to
work with. But they shouldn't be hyphenated either, for the same
reasons.

::: calibre9
:::

Word processors have an annoying default habit of making every web and
e-mail address underlined and blue. That might make some sense if you're
creating a PDF that needs to include hyperlinks. But it makes no sense
at all if you're creating a document that needs to be printed.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to turn off automatic hyperlinks**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹AutoCorrect Options›* ➤ *‹AutoFormat
> As You Type›* ➤ uncheck *‹Internet and network paths with
> hyperlinks›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007** \| Office menu ➤ *‹Word Options›* ➤ *‹Proofing›* ➤
> *‹AutoCorrect Options›* ➤ *‹AutoFormat As You Type›* ➤ uncheck
> *‹Internet and network paths with hyperlinks›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2010** \| *‹File›* ➤ *‹Options›* ➤ *‹Proofing›* ➤
> *‹AutoCorrect Options›* ➤ *‹AutoFormat As You Type›* ➤ uncheck
> *‹Internet and network paths with hyperlinks›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹QuickCorrect›* ➤ *‹SpeedLinks›* ➤
> uncheck *‹Format words as hyperlinks when you type them›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Pages›* ➤ *‹Preferences›* ➤ *‹Auto-Correction›* ➤
> uncheck *‹Automatically detect e-mail and web addresses›*]{.calibre7}

[[Text formatting](#calibre_link-146) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-146)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[small caps]{.bold1}

[[Use real small caps; avoid fakes]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Small caps* are short capital letters designed to blend with lowercase
text. They're usually slightly taller than lowercase letters.

::: calibre9
:::

I'm a big fan of small caps. They look great and they're very useful as
an alternative to [bold or italic](#calibre_link-44) or [all
caps](#calibre_link-45).

::: calibre9
:::

But most people have never seen real small caps. They've only seen the
ersatz small caps that word processors generate when small-cap
formatting is used.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000038.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Small-cap formatting works by scaling down regular caps. But compared to
the other characters in the font, the fake small caps that result are
too tall, and their vertical strokes are too light. The color and height
of real small caps have been calibrated to blend well with the normal
uppercase and lowercase letters.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Typographers use the word **color** to refer to how light or dark a
> text block appears on a page (even if all the characters are
> black).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Therefore, two rules for small caps:

::: calibre9
:::

1.  Don't click on the small-cap formatting box in your word processor.
    Ever. This option does not produce small caps. It produces inferior
    counterfeits.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  The rules for [all caps](#calibre_link-45) also apply to small caps:
    use small caps sparingly, add [letterspacing](#calibre_link-49), and
    turn on [kerning](#calibre_link-48).

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Now for the bad news. If you want real small caps, you'll have to buy
them --- they're not included with [Times New Roman](#calibre_link-108)
or any other [system font](#calibre_link-43).

::: calibre9
:::

Usually, small caps come in their own font file that shows up separately
in the font menu. When you want small caps, you format the text with the
small-cap font. You can also use [paragraph and character
styles](#calibre_link-80) to apply small caps, and eliminate most of the
tedium of using a separate font.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ With small caps, it's up to you whether to use regular capital letters
at the beginning of capitalized words.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Even if you get a small-cap font, the small-cap formatting box will
still give you fake small caps. To avoid confusion, just forget the
formatting box exists.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Many new fonts are sold in the OpenType format instead of the older
TrueType format. Font designers prefer OpenType because it allows small
caps to be built into the main font. Unfortunately, word-processor
support for this feature is weak. Neither Word nor WordPerfect supports
OpenType small caps. (Pages does.) So most lawyers will be stuck with
the traditional technique of using a separate small-cap font. If you buy
a professional font family (like the ones shown in [Font
samples](#calibre_link-57)), be sure to find out if you can get the
small caps in a separate TrueType file.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ "Can I get real small caps that match [Times New
Roman](#calibre_link-108)?" Yes you can. Monotype Imaging, the company
that owns Times New Roman, also sells the accompanying small caps. Enter
code [11801](http://typo.la/11801) on the Typography for Lawyers
website.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If you're a recent law-school graduate, you probably remember that law
reviews use small caps in certain footnotes. Don't bring this habit to
work. *Bluebook* rule B1 (19th ed. 2010) forbids practitioners from
using small caps in citations to publications.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1148 .mbppagebreak}

::: calibre9
:::

[hierarchical headings]{.bold1}

[[Consider tiered numbers]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Traditionally, *hierarchical headings* in legal documents start with
roman numerals at the top level (I, II, III); then switch to capital
letters (A, B, C); then to arabic numerals (1, 2, 3); then to lowercase
letters (a, b, c); then to romanettes (i, ii, iii); then to variations
of the above using two parentheses instead of one, or other barely
visible changes.

::: calibre9
:::

This is a terrible way to label hierarchical headings.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  Roman numerals and romanettes stink. They're difficult to read.
    (Quick, what number is XLIX?) They're easy to confuse at a glance.
    (II vs. III, IV vs. VI, XXI vs. XII.) If what we mean by I, II, III
    is 1, 2, 3, then let's just say so.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[Yes, that's what they're called ---
    > **romanettes**.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Letters are not much better because while we immediately recognize
    A, B, C as equivalent to 1, 2, 3, the letter-to-number correlation
    gets weaker as we go past F, G, H. (Quick, what number is T?) If
    what we mean by J, K, L is 10, 11, 12, then let's just say so.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Mixing roman numerals and letters results in ambiguous references
    --- when you see a lowercase *i*, does it denote the first item or
    the ninth item? Does a lowercase *v* denote the fifth item or the
    22nd item?

    ::: calibre9
    :::

4.  By using only one index on each header, it's easy to lose track of
    where you are in the hierarchy. If I'm at subheading *d*, is that
    *d* under superheading 2 or 3?

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Lawyers should take a cue from technical writers, who solved this
problem long ago --- by using tiered numbers as indexes for hierarchical
headings.

::: calibre9
:::

So instead of:

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000039.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

You'd have:

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000040.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

To my eyes, this system is more understandable, because it only uses
numbers. It's also more navigable, because it's always clear from a
tiered number where you are in the hierarchy. And every modern word
processor can automatically produce tiered numbers. Consider it.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1175 .mbppagebreak}
