::: calibre9
:::

[mixing fonts]{.bold1}

[[Less is more]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Enthusiasm for fonts often leads to enthusiasm for multiple fonts, and
then the question: "How do I get better at *mixing fonts* in a
document?"

::: calibre9
:::

Mixing fonts is like mixing patterned shirts and ties --- there aren't
blackletter rules. Some people have a knack for it; some don't.

::: calibre9
:::

For instance: I recently had to attend a two-day lecture. I prefer not
to dignify the speaker's presentation software by mentioning its name.
Suffice it to say that her slides were both powerless and pointless.

::: calibre9
:::

But the slides did run through just about every [system
font](#calibre_link-43), and often multiple fonts per slide. I passed
the time by inventing a system-font drinking game.

::: calibre9
:::

That story is offered as a caution, not a deterrent. I encourage you to
try mixing fonts. It's a useful skill. Learning it will help you develop
your eye for typography.

::: calibre9
:::

Bear in mind these general principles.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  Mixing fonts is never a requirement --- it's an option. You can get
    plenty of mileage out of one font using variations based on [point
    size](#calibre_link-46), [bold or italic](#calibre_link-44), [small
    caps](#calibre_link-53), and so on.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  The rule of diminishing returns applies. Most documents can tolerate
    a second font; many fewer can tolerate a third; almost none can
    tolerate four or more. (If you're making a presentation, treat all
    the slides as one document.)

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  You can mix any two fonts that are identifiably different. If you've
    heard you can only mix a serif font with a sans serif font, it's not
    true. Much like mixing colors, lower contrast between fonts can be
    more effective than higher contrast.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[[This sidebar](#calibre_link-166) explains the difference
    > between serif and sans serif fonts.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    For instance, look at [these four fonts](#calibre_link-167). You
    would never mix Palatino and Palatino Nova --- the differences are
    too subtle. But I could easily see mixing Minion and Arno, even
    though they're both serif fonts. You can also look at any American
    newspaper --- typically, the [body text](#calibre_link-66) and the
    headlines are both in serif fonts, but different ones.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

4.  Font mixing is most successful when each font has a consistent role
    in the document. In a [research memo](#calibre_link-85), try one
    font for body text and one font for [headings](#calibre_link-47). Or
    in a [motion](#calibre_link-84), try one font for things in the
    center of the document (body text and headings) and one font for
    things at the edges (line numbers, footer, and other miscellany). Or
    in [bulleted and numbered lists](#calibre_link-69), try one font for
    the bullet or number and one font for the text of the list item ---
    a technique I use throughout this book.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

5.  It rarely works to have multiple fonts in a single paragraph. Better
    to restrict yourself to one font per paragraph, and change fonts
    only at paragraph breaks.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

6.  While I'm reluctant to endorse rote methods, this one works pretty
    reliably: mix fonts by the same font designer. For instance,
    [Palatino Nova](#calibre_link-167) and [Optima
    Nova](#calibre_link-168), both the work of font designer Hermann
    Zapf, would mix well.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

::: {#calibre_link-1203 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-169 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1204 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1205 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1206 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1207 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1208 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1209 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1210 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1211 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1212 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1213 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1214 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1215 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1216 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1217 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1218 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1219 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1220 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1221 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1222 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1223 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1224 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1225 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1226 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1227 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1228 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1229 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1230 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1231 .mbppagebreak}
![A Brief History of Times New Roman](images/000041.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Times New Roman gets its name from the *Times* of London, the British
newspaper. In 1929, the Times hired typographer Stanley Morison of
Monotype, a British font foundry, to create a new text font. Morison led
the project and supervised Victor Lardent, an advertising artist for the
Times, who drew the letterforms.

::: calibre9
:::

After Monotype completed Times New Roman, it had to license the design
to then-rival Linotype, because the Times used Linotype's typesetting
machines. (Think of Monotype and Linotype as the Depression-era
Microsoft and Apple.) Since then, Monotype has sold the font as "Times
New Roman" and Linotype has marketed its version as "Times Roman."

::: calibre9
:::

Typesetting technology has evolved since then, but due to its enduring
popularity, Times New Roman has always been one of the first fonts
available in each new format. This, in turn, has only increased its
reach.

::: calibre9
:::

In 1984, Apple licensed Times Roman for the Macintosh; in 1992,
Microsoft licensed Times New Roman for Windows. This put the font into
the hands of millions of new users. The number of documents set in Times
New Roman exploded.

::: calibre9
:::

Objectively, there's nothing wrong with Times New Roman. It was designed
for a newspaper, so it's a bit narrower than most text fonts ---
especially the bold style. (Newspapers prefer narrow fonts because they
fit more text per line.) The italic is mediocre. But those aren't fatal
flaws. Times New Roman is a workhorse font that's been successful for a
reason.

::: calibre9
:::

Yet it's an open question whether its longevity is attributable to its
quality or merely to its ubiquity. Helvetica still inspires enough
affection to have been the subject of a 2007 documentary feature. Times
New Roman, meanwhile, has not attracted similar acts of homage.

::: calibre9
:::

Why not? Fame has a dark side. When Times New Roman appears in a book,
document, or advertisement, it connotes apathy. It says, "I submitted to
the font of least resistance." Times New Roman is not a font choice so
much as the absence of a font choice, like the blackness of deep space
is not a color. To look at Times New Roman is to gaze into the void.

::: calibre9
:::

If you have a choice about using Times New Roman, please stop. Use
something else. See [Font samples](#calibre_link-57) for other options.

::: calibre9
:::

Did you make your business cards and letterhead at your local copy shop?
No, you didn't, because you didn't want them to look shoddy and cheap.
If you cared enough to avoid the copy shop, then you care enough to
avoid Times New Roman. Times New Roman connotes apathy. You are not
apathetic.

::: calibre9
:::

If you don't have a choice about using Times New Roman, make Times New
Roman look its best. Both Windows 7 and Mac OS X now ship with
Monotype's Times New Roman. Monotype offers additional styles in the
[Times New Roman family](#calibre_link-170) that will improve its
versatility and appearance, like small caps and additional weights.

::: calibre9
:::

The origin of the Times New Roman design has always been a bit
mysterious. Stanley Morison was certainly familiar with 16th-century
French typographer Robert Granjon, whose work has been said to be a
starting point for Times New Roman.

::: calibre9
:::

But its more direct ancestor is probably Plantin, another Monotype font,
designed in 1914 by Frank Pierpont. Plantin was also based on Granjon's
work. Seen side by side, the resemblance is unmistakable: Times New
Roman is a taller, brighter version of Plantin.

::: calibre9
:::

Or is it? Typographer Mike Parker discovered that in the early 1900s ---
before Times New Roman or Plantin existed --- Boston yacht builder
William Starling Burgess drew samples of a new font and sent them to
Monotype's U.S. affiliate. Burgess lost interest in the project, but his
drawings were never returned. Parker theorizes that years later,
Burgess's drawings were passed along to Morison, who used them as the
basis of Times New Roman.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000042.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Parker's theory is based in part on his examination of Burgess's
drawings, which are archived at the Smithsonian. Parker created a new
font family from these drawings --- and the result is uncannily similar
to Times New Roman. In honor of Burgess, Parker named the font Starling.

::: calibre9
:::

Parker's theory is controversial among some font historians because it
implies that Morison appropriated someone else's work without credit. It
may also be controversial because Parker was Linotype's typographic
director for 20 years.

::: calibre9
:::

But these criticisms are a little silly. Just about every font design is
the product of new ideas mixed with old ideas --- some acknowledged,
some not. As with any creative endeavor, the line between acceptable
influence and unethical appropriation is often subjective.

::: calibre9
:::

Moreover, as time passes and memories fade, it's increasingly unlikely
that the parentage of Times New Roman will ever be conclusively
determined.

::: calibre9
:::

For writers and other font users, the upshot is that good alternatives
to Times New Roman exist that share its essential flavor but avoid its
shortcomings. If you're a diehard fan of Times New Roman, [consider
them](#calibre_link-170). It's never too late to branch out.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1232 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-171 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1233 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1234 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1235 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1236 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1237 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1238 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1239 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1240 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1241 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1242 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1243 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1244 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1245 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1246 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1247 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1248 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1249 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1250 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1251 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1252 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1253 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1254 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1255 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1256 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1257 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1258 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1259 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1260 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1261 .mbppagebreak}
[[Font samples]{.bold1}]{.calibre21}

::: calibre3
:::

Fonts are only one ingredient of typography. And great fonts are neither
necessary nor sufficient for great typography. It's possible to produce
professional-level typography with [system fonts](#calibre_link-43).
It's also possible to produce awful typography with professional fonts.

::: calibre9
:::

That's why this chapter appears in the middle of the book rather than at
the beginning. Don't rely on professional fonts as a substitute for the
fundamentals of [type composition](#calibre_link-13) and [text
formatting](#calibre_link-39). But also don't overlook professional
fonts as an easy, cost-effective typographic upgrade for your documents.

::: calibre9
:::

[**What are fonts?**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

Fonts are not programs that run software code, like your word processor
or web browser. They are static data files, like MP3s or PDFs. Each font
file contains information that defines the shapes of the letters,
spacing, [kerning](#calibre_link-48), and other miscellany. There's one
font file for each style in the family. (A *style* means one visual
variant, like roman, italic, bold, etc.)

::: calibre9
:::

Most professional fonts are delivered in the OpenType format. Some are
offered in the older TrueType format. OpenType and TrueType files can be
used on either Windows or the Mac.

::: calibre9
:::

[**Why use professional fonts?**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

The best professional fonts are better than any system font --- and in
ways that everyone, even people who think they don't have an eye for
typography, can appreciate. Though you can't have the world's best
typographers lay out your documents, you can incorporate their work into
your documents with a font.

::: calibre9
:::

Professional fonts are also a great value. Yes, they cost money. But you
can get a top-quality font family for under \$200. These fonts will
improve the appearance of every document you create, they're
distinctive, they will never break, they won't go obsolete in three
years, and they won't need to be upgraded. Best of all, you can put them
to work without learning anything new.

::: calibre9
:::

[How these samples are organized]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

In this section, I've started with the most common system fonts and
picked professional fonts that would make good substitutes. Each sample
page contains four fonts.

::: calibre9
:::

The system font appears at the top.

::: calibre9
:::

The second font is a baby step --- a professional font that's as close
as possible to the system font, but still a worthwhile improvement.

::: calibre9
:::

The third font is visibly different than the system font but
incorporates similar visual themes.

::: calibre9
:::

The fourth font is even more different, but would still make a good
substitute for the system font.

::: calibre9
:::

Each font has a code attached to it. These codes will take you to PDF
sample documents on the Typography for Lawyers website and information
on how to get the font. To use a code, go to:

::: calibre9
:::

<http://www.typographyforlawyers.com/code/>

::: calibre9
:::

Enter the code number in the box that appears. (The names of the fonts
are also directly linked to these pages.)

::: calibre9
:::

This section is not exhaustive or exclusive. The
[bibliography](#calibre_link-93) includes books that go into more depth
about the kinds of professional fonts available and how they differ from
each other.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to buy fonts**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

Fonts are sold online. You can buy fonts either direct from the websites
of font designers, or from retailers who sell fonts from many designers.
There's not much difference in price, which is in the range of \$20--50
per style. After you pay, you download the fonts and install them.

::: calibre9
:::

For [body text](#calibre_link-66), the core styles you will want are
roman, italic, bold, bold italic, and roman small caps.

::: calibre9
:::

[About font names]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

Font names are confusing, even for professional typographers. Names of
contemporary fonts (e.g., Myriad, Minion) are trademarked, so their
names are distinct. But names of long-dead typographers (e.g.,
Baskerville, Garamond, Caslon) are not protected, and their names get
included in many font names whether the association is apt or not.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Another reason to use the Typography for Lawyers web codes: they're
> guaranteed to take you to the exact fonts mentioned
> here.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The name of a dead typographer says nothing about the quality of the
font nor how it appears on the page. For instance, Stempel Garamond and
ITC Garamond are about as similar as Bart Simpson and Lisa Simpson.

::: calibre9
:::

To further complicate the picture, some fonts with trademarked names
(e.g., Helvetica, Palatino) have been revised and released under
slightly different names (e.g., Helvetica Neue, Palatino Nova). Pay
attention to the full name.

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to install (or remove) fonts**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Windows** \| All versions of Windows since XP use the same
> procedure. Go to the *‹Start›* menu ➤ *‹Control Panel›* ➤ *‹Fonts›*.
> This will open a folder with all your installed fonts. Drag your new
> fonts into this folder. (To remove fonts, delete font files from this
> same folder.)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Mac** \| In the *‹Applications›* folder, launch *‹Font Book›*. Drag
> your new fonts into the font list. (To remove fonts, delete fonts from
> the list.)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[**How to use fonts**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

Once installed, new fonts show up in your font menu along with the usual
system fonts. Use them the same way.

::: calibre9
:::

[**Respect your license**]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

Fonts are software. Like most software, fonts are offered under a
license. Fonts are usually licensed per user. The most common way font
licenses are violated is when someone buys a single-user license and
then shares it with others in the organization.

::: calibre9
:::

Please --- be a good typographic citizen. Buy the number of licenses you
need and follow the license terms.

::: calibre9
:::

[Disclosure]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

I have no ownership stake in any of the fonts shown here, except [FB
Alix](#calibre_link-172) and [Equity](#calibre_link-173).

::: calibre9
:::

I designed FB Alix to fill a need --- which I discovered while writing
this book --- for a monospaced font family optimized for body text. I
own FB Alix, which is published by the Font Bureau.

::: calibre9
:::

Equity is my most recent design, a text face for legal writing. Equity
will be released in November 2011.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1262 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-174 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1263 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-1264}![Arial](images/000043.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[When sans serif fonts emerged in the 19th century, they were used
mostly at headline sizes, not [body text](#calibre_link-66) sizes. But
modern typography uses sans serifs at all sizes, and designs like
**Arial** ([code 11601](http://typo.la/11601)) and Helvetica were meant
to be comfortable for extended reading. I detest Arial (go
[here](#calibre_link-175) to read why), but **ITC Franklin Gothic**
([code 11602](http://typo.la/11602)), **Univers** ([code
11603](http://typo.la/11603)), and **Syntax** ([code
11604](http://typo.la/11604)) are excellent alternatives. All three are
well-established sans serif fonts that are familiar but not
mundane.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-176 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1265 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-150}![Helvetica](images/000044.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Helvetica** ([code 11701](http://typo.la/11701)) and Univers are
examples of what I would call "neutral" sans serif fonts, designed to be
plain and informational. If you like Helvetica, try **Helvetica Neue**
([code 11702](http://typo.la/11702)), a revision that addressed many
subtle design flaws in the original. If you're less attached to
Helvetica, try **Frutiger** ([code 11703](http://typo.la/11703)), a
classic and influential sans serif design. **Myriad** ([code
11704](http://typo.la/11704)) was inspired by Frutiger, and has become
the high-tech Helvetica --- *Wired* magazine used it extensively before
it became Apple's corporate font.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-177 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1266 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-170}![Times New Roman](images/000045.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[If you like good old **Times New Roman** ([code
11801](http://typo.la/11801)), investigate the other styles (e.g.,
semibold, small caps) available from Monotype Imaging. **Plantin**
([code 11802](http://typo.la/11802)) and **Starling** ([code
11803](http://typo.la/11803)) are based on the work of two designers who
likely influenced Times New Roman (more about that
[here](#calibre_link-56)). Compared to Times New Roman, Plantin is less
compressed horizontally, and Starling has an appealing hand-finished
quality. **Tiempos** ([code 11805](http://typo.la/11805)) combines
elements of both Plantin and Times New Roman, but adds a sharp,
contemporary finish.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-178 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1267 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-167}![Palatino](images/000046.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Palatino** ([code 11901](http://typo.la/11901)) is the work of
Hermann Zapf, who is a calligrapher by training. Many of his fonts
reflect this influence. But the Palatino [system font](#calibre_link-43)
is a harsh representation of Zapf's original design. **Palatino Nova**
([code 11902](http://typo.la/11902)) is Zapf's own reworking of Palatino
that restores its original fluid subtlety. **Minion** ([code
11903](http://typo.la/11903)) and **Arno** ([code
11904](http://typo.la/11904)) are also influenced by pen lettering.
Minion is a clean design, widely used for books. Arno is darker and more
distinctive.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-179 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1268 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-172}![Courier New](images/000047.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[I'm in an awkward position. As your typography advisor, I've counseled
you not to use [monospaced fonts](#calibre_link-42). But the truth is
--- I really like them. The golden age of monospaced fonts was probably
the 1950s, when IBM led the typewriter industry and released a series of
great monospaced designs. One of these was Courier. But the system font
**Courier New** ([code 12001](http://typo.la/12001)) is a beastly
imitation of the original: spindly, lumpy, and just plain ugly.
**Courier 10 Pitch** ([code 12002](http://typo.la/12002)) is a better
rendition of the original Courier design. In honor of this book, I've
created **FB Alix** ([code 12003](http://typo.la/12003)), a monospaced
font family influenced by several typewriter fonts of the '50s, and
optimized for [body text](#calibre_link-66). FB Alix has a feature
that's very rare among monospaced fonts: a genuine italic, instead of a
sloped roman like Courier.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-180 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1269 .mbppagebreak}
![Georgia](images/000048.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Georgia** ([code 12101](http://typo.la/12101)) was designed primarily
to work well on screen. The compromise is that it can be clunky on the
printed page. **Miller** ([code 12102](http://typo.la/12102)) shares
Georgia's DNA but it's more subtle and detailed, making it preferable to
Georgia for print use. (See a larger-size comparison
[here](#calibre_link-181).) Like Miller, **Ingeborg** ([code
12103](http://typo.la/12103)) and **Lyon** ([code
12104](http://typo.la/12104)) are based on historical models but with a
contemporary spin, making them a nice blend of traditional and
modern.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-182 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1270 .mbppagebreak}
![Verdana](images/000049.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Verdana** ([code 12201](http://typo.la/12201)) was designed for the
screen, so it's great for websites. For printed material, not so great.
**Gotham** ([code 12202](http://typo.la/12202)), **Amplitude** ([code
12203](http://typo.la/12203)), and **Verlag** ([code
12204](http://typo.la/12204)) are sans serif fonts that work well in
print. Gotham and Verlag are known as *geometric* sans serif fonts
because they're built on basic mathematical shapes. Verlag is used in
this book for sidebar text. Amplitude draws from the tradition of
*agates*, newspaper fonts designed to be readable at very small sizes
--- that's why I use it on my [business
cards](#calibre_link-87).]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-183 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1271 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-152}![Gill Sans](images/000050.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[I complain a lot about [system fonts](#calibre_link-43) but I won't say
a bad word about **Gill Sans** ([code 12301](http://typo.la/12301)) ---
it's overexposed, but it hasn't worn out its welcome. (With me, at
least.) Gill Sans lit the way for other sans serif fonts that combine
geometric precision with looser hand-drawn features, though many fail to
find the right balance. **Mr Eaves** ([code
12302](http://typo.la/12302)), **FF Scala Sans** ([code
12303](http://typo.la/12303)), and **FF Quadraat Sans** ([code
12304](http://typo.la/12304)) are successful.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-184 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1272 .mbppagebreak}
![Cambria](images/000051.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[It's counterintuitive, but a well-designed font can have a lot of
subtle variation between letters and still look consistent on the page.
The converse of this principle is that a font with too much consistency
can be numbing to read. **Cambria** ([code 12401](http://typo.la/12401))
is an example of this problem. It works well on screen, but on the
printed page, it induces headaches. **Guardian** ([code
12402](http://typo.la/12402)) and **Brioni** ([code
12403](http://typo.la/12403)) are similar to Cambria but avoid monotony.
**FF Tisa** ([code 12404](http://typo.la/12404)) has a more relaxed,
informal rhythm.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-185 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1273 .mbppagebreak}
![Calibri](images/000052.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Calibri** ([code 12501](http://typo.la/12501)) does its job better
than Cambria, though it's still better on screen than in print. The
rounded corners of the letters make text printed in Calibri seem soft.
For a clean, slightly narrow sans serif font, you have better options.
**FF Meta** ([code 12502](http://typo.la/12502)) has been hugely popular
since its release, for good reason: it's versatile and attractive.
**News Gothic** ([code 12503](http://typo.la/12503)) is a classic design
from the early 1900s. **Vista Sans** ([code
12504](http://typo.la/12504)) has a very 21st-century look, and also has
a serif sibling called [Vista Slab](#calibre_link-186).]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-187 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1274 .mbppagebreak}
![Baskerville](images/000053.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[John Baskerville was an 18th-century English typographer whose work has
inspired many fonts. But the **Baskerville** ([code
12601](http://typo.la/12601)) system font is mediocre: brittle and
excessively quaint. The best recreation of the traditional Baskerville
look is **Baskerville 10** ([code 12602](http://typo.la/12602)). The
definitive modernist reinterpretation is **Mrs Eaves** ([code
12603](http://typo.la/12603)). William Caslon was a contemporary of
Baskerville whose fonts had a similar heft and authority. **Williams
Caslon** ([code 12604](http://typo.la/12604)) captures these qualities
well.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-188 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1275 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-173}![Hoefler Text](images/000054.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Hoefler Text** ([code 12701](http://typo.la/12701)) is perfectly
nice, but the version included with the Mac omits the semibold and bold
styles. The publisher, Hoefler & Frere-Jones, offers the full family.
**Stempel Garamond** ([code 12703](http://typo.la/12703)) is my favorite
"Garamond" font. I'm also a fan of **Sabon** ([code
12704](http://typo.la/12704)) --- its wide, legible italic makes
citations look great. But my preferred font for legal writing is
**Equity** ([code 12705](http://typo.la/12705)), a new text family I've
designed to suit the particular needs of lawyers. Equity combines the
look of a traditional European text face with contemporary
details.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-189 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1276 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-168}![Optima](images/000055.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Optima** ([code 12801](http://typo.la/12801)), like Palatino, is the
work of Hermann Zapf, and also shows the influence of pen-drawn letters.
**Optima Nova** ([code 12802](http://typo.la/12802)) is Zapf's own
reworking of the design, with improvements that include better italic
styles --- note the differences between the a, e, f, and g. **Amira**
([code 12803](http://typo.la/12803)) is a recent design also influenced
by pen lettering, though with a more angular feel than Optima. Amira is
used in this book for captions. **Magma** ([code
12804](http://typo.la/12804)) is a hybrid of the pen-drawn and sans
serif models, and it's jauntier than Optima and Amira.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-190 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1277 .mbppagebreak}
[]{#calibre_link-186}![Rockwell](images/000056.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[**Rockwell** ([code 12901](http://typo.la/12901)) is an example of a
*slab-serif* font, so named because the serifs on the ends of the
letters are thick and rectangular. Rockwell's not bad, but it's a little
dated. Modern slab-serif designs are more subtle and versatile.
**Sentinel** ([code 12902](http://typo.la/12902)) is an updated version
of the classic slab-serif look. **Vista Slab** ([code
12904](http://typo.la/12904)), on the other hand, is assertively
contemporary. **Silica** ([code 12903](http://typo.la/12903)) falls in
between. Slab serifs work well for display and short stretches of text.
They also mix well with regular serif fonts, for example as
[headings](#calibre_link-47).]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-191 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1278 .mbppagebreak}
![Eldorado](images/000057.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[A few more [body text](#calibre_link-66) fonts worth knowing about.
**Eldorado** ([code 13001](http://typo.la/13001)) is a lively design
with an unusual feature: a separate "micro" style for very small text,
like footnotes. **FF Quadraat** ([code 13002](http://typo.la/13002)) is
a little like raw oysters: an acquired taste, but once you've tried it,
there's nothing else like it. I'm a fan. **Mercury** ([code
13003](http://typo.la/13003)) was designed to withstand the rigors of
high-volume printing. **Bembo Book** ([code
11804](http://typo.la/11804)) has its roots in Italian Renaissance
typography. It has a stately gait and an attractive italic.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

:::::: {#calibre_link-192 .calibre}
::: calibre9
:::

:::: {#calibre_link-1279 .mbppagebreak}
![Bad Fonts](images/000058.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[And finally, some fonts to avoid. **Bodoni** has very high contrast and
isn't appealing at [body text](#calibre_link-193) sizes. **Bookman**
evokes the Ford administration. If fonts were clothing, this would be
the corduroy suit. **Copperplate** is a novelty design that's overstayed
its welcome by forty or fifty years. **Free fonts** like Liberation
Serif (shown above) fill a practical need for fonts without licensing
restrictions. But in terms of design and craftsmanship, you usually get
what you pay for.]{.calibre7}
::::
::::::

::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-194 .calibre}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1280 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout]{.bold1}]{.calibre21}

::: calibre3
:::

Type composition was about picking the right characters. Text formatting
was about the visual appearance of those characters. Page layout is
about the positioning and relationship of text and other elements on the
page.

::: calibre9
:::

In fine printing, typographers usually get to choose the page size of
their documents. But you don't --- most of the documents a lawyer
creates will be on standard printer paper.

::: calibre9
:::

That's no reason to accept mediocrity. English artist and printer
William Morris famously rebelled against mechanized, mass-produced
typography --- in the 1890s. He went on to produce a series of beautiful
books intended as both a rebuke and an antidote to the increasingly
coarse and industrialized ritual of printing.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000059.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[[Above: Page from the []{#calibre_link-270}Kelmscott *Chaucer*, printed
by William Morris, 1896.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Today, the struggle continues. Word processors beckon us with default
settings and templates that promise great results with no effort.

::: calibre9
:::

But you only get out what you put in. Don't accept the defaults. You can
do better.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1281 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::

::::::: {#calibre_link-195 .calibre}
:::::: {#calibre_link-1282 .mbppagebreak}
::::: {#calibre_link-1283 .mbppagebreak}
[[Basic rules]{.bold1}]{.calibre21}

::: calibre3
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1284 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::
::::::
:::::::

:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-196 .calibre}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1285 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1286 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1287 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[centered text]{.bold1}

[[It's boring --- use sparingly]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Centered text* is overused. It's the typographic equivalent of vanilla
ice cream --- safe but boring. It's rare to see text centered in a book,
newspaper, or magazine, except for the occasional headline or title.
Asymmetry is nothing to fear.

::: calibre9
:::

Yet it is feared. So for all the fans of centered text, a poem:

::: calibre9
:::

**An Ode to Centered Text**

::: calibre9
:::

[Centered text is fine when used in limited doses,\
like the name on your [business cards](#calibre_link-87) or
[letterhead](#calibre_link-86).\
Or in documents, you can center major section headers\
like "Introduction," "Argument," and "Conclusion."\
(It may be conventional in your jurisdiction\
to center certain text in court filings.)\
If you enjoy centering text, then\
you should learn to use the\
[hard line break](#calibre_link-32)\
so your lines start\
in sensible\
places.\
OK?]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Whole text blocks, including sentence-length headings in court filings,
should not be centered. Centering makes text blocks difficult to read
because both edges of the text block are uneven. Centered text blocks
are also difficult to align with other page elements. See
[headings](#calibre_link-47) for better options.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1288 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-198 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1289 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1290 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1291 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1292 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[justified text]{.bold1}

[[Acceptable but not mandatory]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Justified text* is spaced so the left and right sides of the text block
both have a clean edge. The usual alternative to justified text is
*left-aligned text*, which has an uneven right edge. Compared to
left-aligned text, justification gives text a cleaner, more formal look.

::: calibre9
:::

Justification works by adding white space between the words in each line
so all the lines are the same length. This alters the ideal spacing of
the font, but in paragraphs of reasonable width it's usually not
distracting.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000060.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

If you're using justified text, you must also turn on
[hyphenation](#calibre_link-67) so you don't get gruesomely large spaces
between words.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000061.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Notice that you can fit more words in less space with hyphenation
> turned on.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Justification is a matter of personal preference. It is not a signifier
of professional typography. For instance, most major U.S. newspapers use
a mix of justified and left-aligned text.

::: calibre9
:::

In my law practice, I almost never justify text. Why's that? The
justification engine in a word processor is rudimentary compared to a
professional page-layout program. I find that word-processor
justification can make text look clunky and coarse. Left-aligning the
text is more reliable.

::: calibre9
:::

Also, I don't like my documents to look dry and uninviting, even if the
subject matter is. Left-aligning the text relaxes the page.

::: calibre9
:::

But the choice is yours.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1293 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-199 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1294 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1295 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1296 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1297 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1298 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[first-line indents]{.bold1}

[[Between one and four times the point size]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

A first-line indent is the most common way to signal the start of a new
paragraph. The other common way is with [space between
paragraphs](#calibre_link-62).

::: calibre9
:::

First-line indents and space between paragraphs have the same
relationship as belts and suspenders. You only need one to get the job
done. Using both is a mistake. If you use a first-line indent on a
paragraph, don't use space between. And vice versa.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[A first-line indent on the first paragraph of any text is optional,
> because it's obvious where the paragraph starts. **Drop caps** are
> another option for the first paragraph --- the first letter of the
> paragraph is enlarged so it descends three or four lines into the
> paragraph. Drop caps look pretentious and dorky in legal documents.
> You've been warned.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Typically, a first-line indent should be no smaller than the [point
size](#calibre_link-46) of the text, otherwise it'll be hard to see. The
indent should be no bigger than four times the point size, otherwise the
first line will seem disconnected from the left edge of the text block.
So a paragraph set in 12 point should have a first-line indent of 12--48
points. (Recall that there are 72 points to an inch, so this works out
to 0.17--0.67 inches.)

::: calibre9
:::

But use your judgment --- consider the width of the text block when
setting the first-line indent. For instance, narrow text blocks (three
inches or less) should have first-line indents toward the low end of
this range. Wider text blocks should have bigger indents.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

> The process server was denied entry to\
> the defendant's home in the gated com-\
> munity known as Luxuria.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

> The process server was denied\
> entry to the defendant's home in the gat-\
> ed community known as Luxuria.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

> The process server was denied entry\
> to the defendant's home in the gated com-\
> munity known as Luxuria.

::: calibre9
:::

Don't use [word spaces](#calibre_link-29) or [tabs](#calibre_link-31) to
indent the first line --- as you recall from [white-space
characters](#calibre_link-28), that's not what they're for. Paragraphs
indented with word spaces or tabs are hard to keep consistent and
difficult to reformat. Use the right tool for the job.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to set a first-line indent]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤
> *‹Indents and Spacing›*. Under *‹Indentation›*, from the popup menu
> labeled *‹Special›*, select *‹First line›* and enter the measurement
> in the adjacent box.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Format›*. In the
> box next to *‹First line indent›*, enter the measurement.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹Tabs›*. Under *‹Paragraph Indents›*, in the box
> above *‹First Line›*, enter the measurement.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ It's possible to set a negative first-line indent, or *hanging
indent*. Hanging indents are used in lists to create a rectangular text
block with a list bullet that dangles off to the left. Avoid using a
hanging indent without a bullet --- your text block should not resemble
Oklahoma. Text should only be indented inward.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1299 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-200 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1300 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1301 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1302 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1303 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1304 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1305 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[space between paragraphs]{.bold1}

[[Between four and ten points]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Space between paragraphs* is an alternative to a [first-line
indent](#calibre_link-61) for signaling the start of a new paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

The worst way to put space between paragraphs is to insert an extra
carriage return. (See [carriage returns](#calibre_link-33) if you've
forgotten why.)

::: calibre9
:::

As with first-line indents, you want the space to be large enough to be
easily noticed, but not so large that the paragraphs seem disconnected.
Four to ten points of space (0.06--0.14 inches) will usually suffice.
The larger the [point size](#calibre_link-46), the more space you'll
need between paragraphs to make a visible difference.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to set space between paragraphs]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤
> *‹Indents and Spacing›*. Under *‹Spacing›*, in the box next to
> *‹After›*, enter the measurement.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Format›* ➤
> *‹Spacing between paragraphs›*. Select the button labeled *‹Distance
> in points›*, and enter the measurement in the box.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹Text›*. Under *‹After Paragraph›*, use the
> slider or the adjacent box to enter the measurement.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Is space before a paragraph equivalent to space after? Pretty much,
because when you put two paragraphs of the same kind together, the total
space between them equals the space after the first paragraph plus the
space before the second paragraph. I prefer to use space after, and
reserve space before for special circumstances. For instance, a [block
quotation](#calibre_link-68) may need space before and after to look
vertically aligned.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Space between paragraphs is not the best choice for court filings with
[line numbers](#calibre_link-78). The only way to preserve the vertical
alignment is to make the space between paragraphs equal to a whole line
space. This leaves a lot of big gaps in the page and eats up your page
limits. It can work with [headings](#calibre_link-47) because a document
contains fewer of them.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1306 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-201 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1307 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1308 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1309 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1310 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1311 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1312 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1313 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[line spacing]{.bold1}

[[120--145% of the point size]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Line spacing* is the vertical distance between lines of text. Most
lawyers use either double-spaced lines or single-spaced lines ---
nothing in between.

::: calibre9
:::

These habits are held over from the typewriter era. Originally, a
typewriter's carriage could only move vertically in units of a single
line. Therefore, line-spacing choices were limited to one, two, or more
lines at a time. Double-spacing became the default because single-spaced
typewritten text is dense and hard to read. But double-spacing is still
looser than optimal.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[The traditional term for line spacing is **leading** (rhymes with
> **bedding**). Sometimes you see this term in typesetting software.
> Leading is so named because traditional print shops put strips of lead
> between lines of type to increase vertical
> space.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Most courts adopted their line-spacing standards in the typewriter era.
That's why court rules usually call for double-spaced lines. (Notable
exception: the United States Supreme Court.)

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000062.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Word processors have a bewildering number of ways to set line spacing.
Don't be thrown off --- it all comes back to the same thing.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[If you prefer setting line height in inches rather than points,
> divide the point measurement by 72 (there are 72 points to an
> inch).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[How to set line spacing]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* from the
> menu. Go to the menu under *‹Line spacing›*. *‹Exactly›* is best ---
> enter a fixed measurement. *‹Single›*, *‹1.5 lines›*, and *‹Double›*
> are equivalent to about 117%, 175%, and 233% line spacing, contrary to
> what their names suggest. Don't use these --- they miss the target
> zone of 120--145%. *‹Multiple›* is also acceptable --- enter line
> spacing as a decimal. To get line spacing in the 120--145% range, use
> a *‹Multiple›* value of 1.03--1.24. (Not 1.20--1.45 --- as noted
> above, Word uses peculiar line-spacing math.) Never use *‹At least›*,
> because that gives Word permission to adjust your line spacing
> unpredictably.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Line›* ➤ *‹Height›* and
> *‹Spacing›*. Line spacing in WordPerfect is the *‹Height›* value
> multiplied by the *‹Spacing›* value. (The benefit of this complication
> may be appreciated by WordPerfect fans, but is lost on me.) I
> recommend always leaving *‹Spacing›* at 1.0 and just setting your line
> spacing with *‹Height›*. Selecting *‹Height›* lets you choose from
> *‹Fixed›* or *‹At Least›*. Use *‹Fixed›* --- enter a measurement in
> the 120--145% range. Don't use *‹At Least›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹Text›*. Under *‹Spacing›* and *‹Line›*, there's
> a slider. Find the popup menu next to the slider. *‹Exactly›* is best
> --- enter a fixed measurement. You can also use the *‹Multiple›*
> option, but like Word, it adds extra space --- about 17%. Therefore,
> to get line spacing in the 120--145% range, use a *‹Multiple›* value
> of 1.03--1.29. Avoid the other options.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Recall that different fonts set at the same point size may not appear
the same size on the page. (See [point size](#calibre_link-46) for why.)
A side effect is that fonts that run small will need less line spacing,
and vice versa.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Line spacing has a much more significant effect on the length of a
document than point size. If you need to fit a document onto a certain
number of pages, try adjusting the line spacing first.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1314 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-202 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1315 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1316 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1317 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1318 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1319 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1320 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1321 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1322 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[line length]{.bold1}

[[45--90 characters or 2--3 alphabets]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Line length* is the distance between the left and right edges of a text
block. Overly long lines are a common problem, but they're easy to
correct. Shorter lines will make a big difference in the legibility and
professionalism of your layout.

::: calibre9
:::

The most useful way to measure line length is by average characters per
line. Measuring in inches or centimeters is less useful because the
[point size](#calibre_link-46) of the font affects the number of
characters per inch. Average characters per line works independently of
point size.

::: calibre9
:::

Shorter lines are more comfortable to read than longer lines. As line
length increases, your eye has to travel farther from the end of one
line to the beginning of the next, making it harder to track your
progress vertically.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Newspaper columns are very tall, so they also have to be very narrow
> to make vertical tracking easy.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Aim for an average line length of 45--90 characters, including spaces.
You can check line length using word count.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to use word count]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹Word Count›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Review›* ➤ *‹Proofing›* panel ➤ *‹Word
> Count›* (icon looks like "ABC" above "123")]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹Word Count›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Edit›* ➤ *‹Writing Tools›* ➤ *‹Show Statistics›*. If
> text is selected in the document, the *‹Range›* popup menu lets you
> toggle between word counts for the selection and for the whole
> document.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Alternatively, use the alphabet test to set line length. You should be
able to fit between two and three alphabets on your line, like so:

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000063.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1323 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-203 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1324 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1325 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1326 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1327 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1328 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1329 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1330 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1331 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1332 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[page margins]{.bold1}

[[One inch is not enough]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Page margins* set the default territory your text occupies on the page.
Page margins determine the width of the text block, and thus have the
greatest effect on [line length](#calibre_link-64). ([Point
size](#calibre_link-46) also affects line length, though more finely.)
As page margins increase, line length decreases, and vice versa.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[A **gutter** margin is extra space on one side of a page that
> accounts for a binding. In a duplex (two-sided) document, the gutter
> will automatically alternate sides.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Most word processors default to page margins of one inch. On standard
8.5″× 11″ paper, that produces a line length of 6.5 inches. That's fine
for [monospaced fonts](#calibre_link-42), which use a lot of horizontal
space. But for proportional fonts, one-inch margins are too small.

::: calibre9
:::

At 12 point, left and right page margins of 1.5--2.0 inches will usually
give you a comfortable line length. But don't take that range as an
absolute --- focus on getting the number of characters per line into the
right range (see [line length](#calibre_link-64)). The smaller the point
size, the larger the page margins will need to be, and vice versa.

::: calibre9
:::

[Fear of white space]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

"But if I use bigger margins, won't a lot of the page be empty?" Sure.
Is that a problem?

::: calibre9
:::

The 8.5″× 11″ paper size is a standard imposed on us by history and
tradition. It is arbitrary. It does not represent anyone's idea of a
convenient size for good typography.

::: calibre9
:::

Here's the proof. Are there any publications that use 8.5″× 11″ paper?
Yes, it's the approximate size of many magazines. But do any of those
magazines run text in a single block on the page with one-inch margins?
No --- never. They use multiple-column layouts or find other ways to
divide the page.

::: calibre9
:::

So are there any publications that do run text in a single block on the
page? Sure --- books are usually set in a single column. But do you ever
see a book printed on 8.5″× 11″ paper? No --- never. It would be too big
for comfortable reading.

::: calibre9
:::

Professional typographers never use 8.5″× 11″ paper with a 6.5-inch line
length. Neither should you. Set your text according to the principles of
good typography. The white space will take care of itself. The pleasure
of reading an effectively designed document will soon outweigh the
unfamiliarity of extra white space around the edges.

::: calibre9
:::

"But with those big margins, I won't get nearly as many words on the
page." Let's address that fear with an exercise that brings together
some of what you've learned so far.

::: calibre9
:::

[Typographic exercise]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Start a new document in your word processor. Paste in a text of at
    least 1000 words.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Format this new document as follows: page margins of one inch per
    side, font is [Times New Roman](#calibre_link-108), [point
    size](#calibre_link-46) is 12, [line spacing](#calibre_link-63) is
    "Double" (if you're using Word; if not, use exactly 28 points),
    [first-line indent](#calibre_link-61) is half an inch, and no space
    between paragraphs. I'll call this document A.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[With a 12-point font, "Double" line spacing in Word works out to
    > be 28 points. (If that seems like peculiar math, I agree. See
    > [line spacing](#calibre_link-63).) I'm using it here because it's
    > the line-spacing option many people select by
    > default.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Start another new document in your word processor. Paste in the same
    text.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

4.  Format this second document as follows: page margins of two inches
    per side, font is still Times New Roman, point size is 11, line
    spacing is exactly 15 points, first-line indent is still half an
    inch, and still no space between paragraphs. I'll call this document
    B.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

5.  Print both documents. Which one looks more like a professionally
    typeset book: A or B?

    ::: calibre9
    :::

6.  Which document is more comfortable to read: A or B?

    ::: calibre9
    :::

7.  Which document contains more words per page: A or B? Hint: use word
    count. See [line length](#calibre_link-64) for instructions.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[Shortcut: the "before" and "after" samples in [research
    > memo](#calibre_link-85) are pretty close to the formatting of
    > documents A and B.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

I'm guessing you answered B to the last three questions. If so, you're
seeing how good typography can be a benevolent force --- it improves the
appearance and legibility of your text with no compromise in words per
page.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Do margins all have to be the same size? No. To fit more text on the
page, reduce the top and bottom margins. Your line length will stay the
same, but you'll get more lines per page. To make the text block appear
centered vertically, try making the bottom margin about a quarter-inch
larger than the top margin. (An old typographer's trick --- otherwise,
the text block can look like it's sagging.) Finally, there's no rule
that a text block has to be centered on the page horizontally. For an
asymmetric layout, make the difference between the left and right
margins at least one inch --- weak asymmetry will just look like a
layout error.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The best way to judge a layout is with your eyes, not with a
calculator. But typographers have long enjoyed fiddling with layouts
that incorporate specific mathematical ratios. Most famous among these
is the *golden ratio*, which is approximately 1.618 : 1. If you set page
margins of 2.23 inches on all four sides of 8.5″× 11″ paper, the
proportions of your text block will be close to the golden ratio.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1333 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1335 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1336 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1337 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1338 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1339 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1340 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1341 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1342 .mbppagebreak}

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic

::: calibre9

[body text]{.bold1}

[[Four important considerations]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

**Body text\*\**
is the most common\
element of a document.\
Therefore, how the body\
text looks will have the\
most noticeable effect\
on the appearance\
of the document.\
Consequently,\
you should\
set up the\
body text\
**first.**

::: calibre9
:::

Start with\
font, [point size](#calibre_link-46),\
[line spacing](#calibre_link-63), and\
[line length](#calibre_link-64), because\
those four decisions will\
largely determine how\
the body text will\
look. OK?

::: calibre9
:::

> [[**\*** Meaning, the text that's the main content of your
> document.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ While I'll stop short of calling it a rule, I strongly recommend using
a serif font --- not a sans serif font --- for body text. Most books,
newspapers, and magazines use serif fonts for body text. It's the
traditional choice and still the best choice.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1344 .mbppagebreak}
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1345 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1350 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1352 .mbppagebreak}

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic

::: calibre9
:::

[hyphenation]{.bold1}

[[Mandatory for justified text; optional
otherwise]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Hyphenation* is the automated process of breaking words between lines
to create more consistency across a text block.

::: calibre9
:::

In [justified text](#calibre_link-60), hyphenation is mandatory.

::: calibre9
:::

In left-aligned text, hyphenation evens the irregular right edge of the
text, called the *rag*. Hyphenation is optional for left-aligned text
because the rag will still be somewhat irregular, even with hyphenation.
Hyphenation doesn't improve text legibility. Consider turning it off.

::: calibre9
:::

As [line length](#calibre_link-64) gets shorter, hyphenation becomes
essential. Why? With hyphenation off, your word processor can only break
lines at word spaces. As the lines get shorter, there are fewer words
and hence fewer possible break points, making awkward breaks more
likely.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to turn hyphenation on (or off)]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹Language›* ➤ *‹Hyphenation›* ➤ check
> (or uncheck) *‹Automatically hyphenate document›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Page Layout›* ➤ *‹Page Setup›* panel ➤
> *‹Hyphenation›* ➤ *‹Automatic›* (or *‹None›*)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| Select all text. *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹Language›* ➤
> *‹Hyphenation›* ➤ check (or uncheck) *‹Turn hyphenation
> on›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ document icon (first
> icon in the top row) ➤ *‹Document›* ➤ check (or uncheck)
> *‹Hyphenate›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Sometimes you may want to suppress automatic hyphenation. For instance,
[headings](#calibre_link-47) are relatively short, so hyphenation often
causes more problems than it solves.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[WRONG:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> B. This Court has personal jurisdic-\
> tion over MegaCorp because its head-\
> quarters are in San Luis Obispo.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[RIGHT:]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}\
> B. This Court has personal\
> jurisdiction over MegaCorp because\
> its headquarters are in San Luis Obispo.

::: calibre9
:::

Hyphenation can be suppressed in a single paragraph, or a set of
paragraphs, if you suppress hyphenation within [paragraph and character
styles](#calibre_link-80).

::: calibre9
:::

[How to suppress hyphenation in a paragraph]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Line
> and Page Breaks›* ➤ *‹Don't hyphenate›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| Select the paragraph. *‹Tools›* ➤ *‹Language›* ➤
> *‹Hyphenation›* ➤ uncheck *‹Turn hyphenation on›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹More›* ➤ *‹Remove hyphenation for
> paragraph›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ You can exert rudimentary control over automatic hyphenation. If
you're curious, search your help file for "hyphenation options." Over
the years, I've never touched these, so I doubt you'll need to either.
The [nonbreaking hyphen](#calibre_link-35) and the [optional
hyphen](#calibre_link-36) solve most hyphenation problems, and even
those are pretty rare.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1356 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-206 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1357 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1358 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1359 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1360 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1361 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1362 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1363 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1364 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1365 .mbppagebreak}

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[block quotations]{.bold1}

[[Don't go on and on]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Formatting *block quotations* is not hard. Reduce the [point
size](#calibre_link-46) and [line spacing](#calibre_link-63) slightly.
Indent the text block between half an inch and a full inch on the left
side, and optionally the same on the right. As with [first-line
indents](#calibre_link-61), make the side indents large enough to be
noticed, but not so large that the [line length](#calibre_link-64) is
too short. Don't put quotation marks at the ends --- they're redundant.

::: calibre9
:::

Block quotations are sometimes unavoidable. If a dispute involves the
interpretation of an agreement, accuracy may demand extensive quoting.

::: calibre9
:::

But pay attention to length. Lawyers sometimes put voluminous material
into a block quotation intending to signal "Hey, I quoted a lot of this
source because it's really important!" The actual signal a reader often
gets is "Hey, I didn't write any of this, I just cribbed it from
somewhere else!" The reader's next thought is usually "Great, I can skip
this," or "What does this have to do with this case?"

::: calibre9
:::

If you want readers to pay attention to quoted material, edit it
carefully and integrate it into the text. Don't just shovel it into a
block quotation.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1369 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::
:::::::::::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-207 .calibre}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1370 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1371 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1372 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1373 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1374 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1375 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1376 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1377 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1378 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1379 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1380 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1381 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1382 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Basic
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[bulleted and numbered lists]{.bold1}

[[Don't type them manually]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Are you still making *bulleted and numbered lists* by manually typing
bullets or numbers at the beginning of each line?

::: calibre9
:::

In the 21st century, no one should be doing this task by hand. Manually
formatted lists are a waste of time and prone to error. Use automated
lists. I can get you started, but if you're unfamiliar with automated
lists, spend some time with your word processor's manual or help file.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to insert an automated list]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**word 2003** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Bullets & Numbering›* ➤ *‹Bulleted›*,
> *‹Numbered›*, or *‹Outline Numbered›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Home›* ➤ *‹Paragraph›* panel. The three
> buttons in the upper-left corner will start a new list: the first
> button starts a bulleted list, the second a numbered list, the third
> an outline list.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Outline/Bullets & Numbering›*. The
> *‹Bullets›* tab offers bulleted lists; the *‹Numbers›* tab offers
> numbered and outline lists.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Open the Styles drawer (command + shift + t). If the
> list styles are not visible, click the list icon in the lower right
> corner of the Styles drawer. Then choose from *‹Bullet›*, *‹Numbered
> List›*, or *‹Harvard›* (an outline list).]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ As I suggested in [mixing fonts](#calibre_link-55), it's acceptable to
set a list index (i.e., a bullet or a number) in a different font than
the list item itself. You can also make the list index a smaller [point
size](#calibre_link-46).

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Asterisks are sometimes used as bullets, but they're not qualified for
the job --- they're too small, and they sit too high.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Look out for overlarge bullets. Like [first-line
indents](#calibre_link-61), they should be big enough to be noticeable,
but no bigger. I usually prefer hollow bullets to solid bullets because
they're more subtle.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ A *dingbat* is a nonalphabetic typographic ornament. That's why those
symbol fonts on your computer have names like Zapf Dingbats and
Wingdings. Dingbat fonts can be a good source of alternative bullets and
numbers. Simple geometric dingbats are better than pictorial ones, which
don't translate to small sizes. Be aware that these dingbat fonts
sometimes contain symbols with cultural or religious significance ---
tread carefully if the symbol is unfamiliar to you.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Word processors use automatic list detection by default. When you type
something that looks like a bulleted or numbered list, it's converted
into an automated list. If that method works for you, great. I turn off
this feature because I find it only guesses right about half the time.

::: calibre9
:::
