[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[tables]{.bold1}

[[The best tool for gridded or complex layouts]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The good news: *tables* are one of the handiest tools in your word
processor. A table is usually the right solution for layout problems
where [white-space characters](#calibre_link-28) aren't up to the task.

::: calibre9
:::

The bad news: tables can be difficult to use. The user interface for
editing them is complex and finicky. While I can't give you a full
tutorial on using tables --- refer to your manual or help file --- I can
give you a few directional tips.

::: calibre9
:::

I've already pointed out the ways in which bad typewriter habits have
endured (e.g., [underlining](#calibre_link-40), [monospaced
fonts](#calibre_link-42)). But the unfortunate truth about word
processors is that their basic model for page layout is similar to that
of a typewriter of a hundred years ago: the document is handled as one
big column of text. That's great if all you need is one big column of
text. It's not so great otherwise.

::: calibre9
:::

A table is useful if you have a spreadsheet-style grid of data. In the
typewriter era, a grid like this would have been handled with
[tabs](#calibre_link-31). These days, you'd use a table.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[These tips about formatting tables apply equally well to
> spreadsheets.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

A table is also useful if text in your layout needs to be positioned
side-by-side or floating at specific locations on the page. (Two common
examples are [letterhead](#calibre_link-86) and [caption
pages](#calibre_link-83).) Making these is frustrating with basic layout
tools but very easy with tables.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to insert a table]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Tables›* panel ➤ *‹Table›* ➤ drag your
> cursor around the grid to set the number of rows and
> columns.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Table›* ➤ *‹Create›* ➤ set the number of rows
> and columns.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Table›*. Pages will give you a four-row,
> three-column table and open the table editor in the Inspector to allow
> further adjustments.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

There are many ways to format a table. But your word processor's default
tables have two formatting defects you should always fix: *cell borders*
and *cell margins*.

::: calibre9
:::

*Cell borders* are the dark lines around each cell in the table. Cell
borders are helpful as guides when you're loading information into the
table. They're less useful once the table is full. The text in the cells
will create an implied grid. Cell borders can make the grid cluttered
and difficult to read, especially in tables with many small cells.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000064.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[You could also make this kind of layout using
> [tabs](#calibre_link-31), but a table is the far better
> choice.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

In this example, cell borders are unnecessary. In other cases, they can
be useful. The goal is to improve the legibility of the table. When
you're ready to format your table, I recommend starting by turning off
all the cell borders and then turning them back on as needed. (See
[rules and borders](#calibre_link-71) for more tips.)

::: calibre9
:::

[How to turn off cell borders]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the table to display menu ➤ *‹Borders and
> Shading›* ➤ *‹Borders›* tab. In the column on the left labeled
> *‹Setting›*, click the button next to *‹None›*. Click
> *‹OK›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| Right-click in the table to display menu ➤
> *‹Borders / Fill›* ➤ *‹Table›* tab. Under *‹Default cell lines›* is
> the label *‹Line›* and a button. Click this button and select the X in
> the upper left corner. Click *‹OK›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| If the cursor is inside a cell, click outside the table.
> Then click the table once to select it. Open the Inspector (command +
> option + i) ➤ table icon in the top row (seventh from left) ➤
> *‹Table›*. Under *‹Cell Borders›* there's a popup menu to set the
> cell-border pattern. Change this to *‹None›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Cell margins* create space between the cell borders and the text of the
cell. Increasing the cell margins is the best way to improve the
legibility of a dense table.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000065.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

The default cell margins, especially in Word, are too tight. With cell
margins, a little goes a long way --- start around 0.03″ and move in
increments of one hundredth of an inch. Also, there's no need to make
the cell margins the same on all sides. The top and bottom margins can
be bigger than the side margins, if that looks right.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to set cell margins]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the table to display menu ➤ *‹Table
> Properties›* ➤ *‹Table›* tab ➤ *‹Options›*. Under *‹Default cell
> margins›*, enter the values. You can also use the up and down arrow
> keys to change the values in increments of 0.01″.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| Select the whole table. Right-click in the table
> to display menu ➤ *‹Format›*. Click the *‹Column›* tab and adjust left
> and right cell margins under *‹Inside margins in column›*. Click the
> *‹Row›* tab and adjust top and bottom cell margins under *‹Row
> margins›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| If the cursor is inside a cell, click outside the table.
> Then click the table once to select it. Open the Inspector (command +
> option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row (fourth from left) ➤ *‹Text›* ➤
> *‹Inset Margin›*. Use the slider to adjust cell margins. Pages only
> lets you set one value that applies to all four sides of each
> cell.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1414 .mbppagebreak}
:::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-210 .calibre}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1415 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1416 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1417 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1418 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1419 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1420 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1421 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1422 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1423 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1424 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1425 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1426 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1427 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1428 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1429 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1430 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[rules and borders]{.bold1}

[[Use sparingly]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

In traditional printing terminology, a *rule* is a line; a *border* is a
box. But in word processors, they're variations of the same function.
Rules and borders can be applied to pages, paragraphs, or
[tables](#calibre_link-70).

::: calibre9
:::

Like [centered text](#calibre_link-59), [bold or
italic](#calibre_link-44), and [all caps](#calibre_link-45), rules and
borders are best used sparingly. Ask yourself: do you really need a rule
or border to make a visual distinction? You can usually get equally good
results by increasing the white space around the text. Try that first.

::: calibre9
:::

For borders, set the thickness between half a point and one point.
Thinner borders can work on professionally printed goods but are too
fine to reproduce well on an office printer. Thicker borders are
counterproductive --- they create noise that upstages the information
inside. You want to see the data, not the lines around the data.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000066.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Similarly, don't use patterned borders (i.e., borders made of anything
other than a single solid line, like dots, dashes, or double lines).
They're unnecessarily complicated.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Edward Tufte coined the word **chartjunk** to describe markings that
> are unnecessary to communicate visual information. Thick grid lines
> are a common kind of chartjunk. Tufte's excellent books on information
> design are included in the
> [bibliography](#calibre_link-93).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

You have more latitude with rules because they don't accumulate the way
borders do. If you want to make a rule thicker than one point or use a
pattern, go ahead. But thick or patterned rules still wear out their
welcome faster than the classic half-point solid rule.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to edit rules & borders]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word 2003** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Borders and Shading›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Home›* ➤ *‹Paragraph›* panel ➤ click the
> arrow on the right edge of the button that looks like a windowpane and
> select *‹Borders and Shading›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Border / Fill›*, or
> for a table, *‹Table›* ➤ *‹Borders / Fill›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹More›*. Use the controls under *‹Border &
> Rules›*. For a table, click the table icon in the Inspector and use
> the controls under *‹Cell Borders›*.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Never make rules and borders out of repeated typographic characters,
like punctuation, [hyphens and dashes](#calibre_link-20), or [math
symbols](#calibre_link-37). Particularly ridiculous is the habit of
using stacked parentheses to make a vertical line on [caption
pages](#calibre_link-83). Not only is it uglier than a vertical rule,
it's much harder to assemble. These habits have been passed down from
typewriters. They're obsolete.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ If you attach a rule to [headings](#calibre_link-47), try putting it
above the heading (rather than below, which is usually the default).
Then the rule is separating the end of the previous section and the
current heading, instead of separating the current heading from its own
section.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1431 .mbppagebreak}

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced

::: calibre9
:::

[widow and orphan control]{.bold1}

[[Your call]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Picture a paragraph that starts at the bottom of one page and continues
at the top of the next page. When only the last line of the paragraph
appears at the top of the next page, that line is called a *widow*. When
only the first line of the paragraph appears at the bottom of the first
page, that line is called an *orphan*.

::: calibre9
:::

*Widow and orphan control* prevents both. Orphans are moved to the next
page with the rest of the paragraph. To cure widows, lines are moved
from the bottom of one page to the top of the next. It's a little more
complicated than it sounds because curing a widow cannot create a new
orphan, nor vice versa.

::: calibre9
:::

Be aware that if you use widow and orphan control, you will frequently
see blank lines at the bottom of your pages. This is normal, since lines
must be transplanted to cure the problem.

::: calibre9
:::

Widow and orphan control in a word processor is all-or-nothing. You
can't control widows and orphans separately, even though widows are more
distracting. Why? Orphans appear at the beginning of a paragraph, so
they're at least a full line. But widows can be any length, even a
single word, because they appear at the end of a paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

Do you need widow and orphan control? Try it. See how it looks. In my
own work, I approach widow and orphan control the same way I approach
[ligatures](#calibre_link-38) --- I only use it if widows and orphans
are causing a visible problem. Otherwise, I find that the blank lines at
the bottom of the page are more annoying than the widows and orphans.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to turn on widow & orphan control]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Line
> and Page Breaks›* ➤ check *‹Widow / Orphan control›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Keep Text Together›* ➤ under
> *‹Widow / Orphan›*, check *‹Prevent the first and last lines of
> paragraphs from being separated across pages›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹More›* ➤ check *‹Prevent widow & orphan
> lines›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ You can also cure isolated widows and orphans with some judicious
editing. But don't use a [hard line break](#calibre_link-32) or
[carriage return](#calibre_link-33).

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1449 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1450 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1451 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1452 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1454 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1456 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1463 .mbppagebreak}

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[keep lines together]{.bold1}

[[Always use with headings]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Keep lines together* ensures that all lines in a paragraph appear on
the same page. If the last line of the paragraph won't fit on the
current page, the whole paragraph gets moved to the next page.

::: calibre9
:::

Use this option with [headings](#calibre_link-47) to prevent them from
starting at the bottom of one page and continuing at the top of the
next. That looks bad.

::: calibre9
:::

Like [widow and orphan control](#calibre_link-72), keeping lines
together will create gaps at the bottom of pages. But unlike widow and
orphan control, you only want to keep lines together in special
situations, not as part of your default text formatting.

::: calibre9
:::

Why? Keeping lines together is a blunter technique. It only works on
whole paragraphs, so the longer the paragraph, the bigger the gap.

::: calibre9
:::

If you need to make groups of elements stick together, the
keep-lines-together option works well with [hard line
breaks](#calibre_link-32). Recall that hard line breaks don't create a
new paragraph, but rather a set of lines. Keeping lines together will
ensure this set of lines appears on a single page.

::: calibre9
:::

For instance, I like to keep lines together in signature blocks:

::: calibre9
:::

> May 19, 2010\
> Butterick Law Corporation\
> \
> By:\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\
> Matthew Butterick\
> Attorney for Plaintiff

::: calibre9
:::

Here, I've put a hard line break at the end of each line. Then I've set
the whole signature block --- which is a single paragraph --- to keep
lines together. That way, I don't have to worry that half the block will
end up on one page and half on the next. All of it travels together.

::: calibre9
:::

Another example: I once had to prepare a jury-instruction form that
required an index of instructions where the judge could enter his
rulings:

::: calibre9
:::

> **CACI 204. Willful suppression of evidence**\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Given as proposed\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Given as modified\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Refused\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Withdrawn

::: calibre9
:::

The problem was that the four choices kept getting split up by page
breaks. The solution was to put hard line breaks after each choice and
keep the lines together to make sure the choices moved as a unit.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to keep lines together]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Line
> and Page Breaks›* ➤ check *‹Keep lines together›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Keep Text Together›* ➤ check *‹Keep
> selected text together on same page›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹More›* ➤ check *‹Keep lines
> together›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1468 .mbppagebreak}

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[keep with next paragraph]{.bold1}

[[Always use with headings]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Keep with next paragraph* binds the last line of a paragraph to the
first line of the next. It ensures no page break happens between the two
paragraphs. It's like [keep lines together](#calibre_link-73), except it
works between paragraphs instead of within a paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

Always use this option with [headings](#calibre_link-47). It looks bad
if a heading appears at the bottom of a page and the text it's
introducing starts on the next page. Keeping with the next paragraph
prevents this.

::: calibre9
:::

The keep-with-next-paragraph option is a little boring on its own. It
gets more interesting when used with its friend, the keep-lines-together
option. For instance, let's return to the jury-instruction example:

::: calibre9
:::

> **CACI 204. Willful suppression of evidence\**
> \_\_\_\_\_ Given as proposed\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Given as modified\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Refused\
> \_\_\_\_\_ Withdrawn

::: calibre9
:::

Here, the name of the jury instruction is one paragraph, and the four
choices below are a second paragraph. The four choices won't get
separated from each other because they're glued together with the
keep-lines-together option. But we don't want the instruction name
getting separated from the choices either. By setting the instruction
name to keep with next paragraph, all five lines are guaranteed to move
as a unit.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Why didn't I make the whole block one paragraph? So I could apply a
> separate paragraph style to the names of the instructions, and
> reformat all of them as a group. (See [paragraph and character
> styles](#calibre_link-80).)]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[How to keep with next paragraph]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Line
> and Page Breaks›* ➤ check *‹Keep with next›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| No direct support for keeping with next paragraph.
> Some say you can approximate the effect with *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Keep Text
> Together›* ➤ *‹Conditional end of page›* ➤ enter the *‹Number of lines
> to keep together›*. I'll leave the rest as an exercise for WordPerfect
> fans.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹More›* ➤ check *‹Keep with following
> paragraph›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1488 .mbppagebreak}
:::

:::::::::::::::::::::::::::

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced

::: calibre9
:::

[page break before]{.bold1}

[[Alternative to hard page breaks]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

*Page break before* ensures that a paragraph starts at the top of a new
page. Visually, there's no difference between using the
page-break-before option and typing a [hard page
break](#calibre_link-34) in front of the paragraph. But that's only
efficient for the occasional paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

The page-break-before option is intended to be incorporated into
[paragraph and character styles](#calibre_link-80) so all paragraphs of
a particular style will start at the top of a new page. For instance,
the top-level headings of a long document --- typing hard page breaks in
front of each heading would be tedious.

::: calibre9
:::

[How to use page break before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Word** \| Right-click in the text and select *‹Paragraph›* ➤ *‹Line
> and Page Breaks›* ➤ check *‹Page break before›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**WordPerfect** \| Not supported. You're stuck using hard page
> breaks.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [**Pages** \| Inspector (command + option + i) ➤ T icon in the top row
> (fourth from left) ➤ *‹More›* ➤ check *‹Paragraph starts on a new
> page›*]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1509 .mbppagebreak}

::: calibre9
:::

[columns]{.bold1}

[[Your call]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

They're unusual in legal documents, but I don't object to *columns* in a
long document like a contract or a settlement agreement. Columns are an
easy way to get a shorter and more legible [line
length](#calibre_link-64) without having to use large margins. On a
standard 8.5″× 11″ page, two or three columns are fine; four is too
many.

::: calibre9
:::

Usually columns look neatest when the rows of text are aligned
vertically between columns. Look at a decent newspaper for an example.
Getting this result takes a little extra effort. Note your [line
spacing](#calibre_link-63) and make sure any [space between
paragraphs](#calibre_link-62) works out to a whole multiple of the line
spacing. The two most common options: set space between paragraphs to
zero, or set it to be the same as the line spacing.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1531 .mbppagebreak}

::: calibre9

[watermarks]{.bold1}

[[Avoid]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

A *watermark* is a notice (e.g., "Draft," "Confidential") that's printed
in the background of a page. Often in six-inch letters. Diagonally. In
red.

::: calibre9
:::

Watermarks interact poorly with the text, creating visual noise that
distracts from reading. (It's a variation of the problem noted in [rules
and borders](#calibre_link-71).) Also, watermarks can darken
considerably when photocopied, aggravating the problem.

::: calibre9
:::

It's hard to make watermarks look good. Better to relocate the watermark
text somewhere inside the header or footer.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1554 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::
::::::::::
:::::::::::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::

::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::

::: calibre9
:::

[line numbers]{.bold1}

[[Exact line spacing is the key]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

If you practice in a jurisdiction that requires court filings with *line
numbers*, you have faced the vexing problem of keeping the line numbers
vertically aligned with the text.

::: calibre9
:::

The secret is simple: use exact [line spacing](#calibre_link-63) with
the line numbers and the [body text](#calibre_link-66).

::: calibre9
:::

California state courts require filings to use double line spacing
(which works out to 28 lines per page), 12-point type, and line numbers
in the left margin. I'll use this as the basic model and you can adapt
it to the rules in your jurisdiction.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[California Rule of Court 2.108(1) also allows filings to use "one
> and one-half" line spacing, which works out to 37 lines per page.
> Strangely, I've never seen a 37-line filing. Is it because California
> lawyers don't know about the rule, or because they don't know how to
> make a 37-line template? (Follow the instructions here but use 18
> points of line spacing instead of 24.)]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

1.  Create a new document with one-inch margins.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  In the body of the document, in 12 point, type a paragraph of about
    five lines that looks like this:

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    HHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHH\
    HHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHH\
    HHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHH\
    HHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHH\
    HHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHH

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  To get true double line spacing, you need to set your line spacing
    to exactly twice the [point size](#calibre_link-46). The H paragraph
    is set in 12 point, so select the whole paragraph and change its
    line spacing to exactly 24 points. Don't use the "Double" line
    spacing option in your word processor --- despite the name, you'll
    end up with line spacing larger than you want. (See [Line
    spacing](#calibre_link-63).)

    ::: calibre9
    :::

4.  Open the document header for editing.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**Word 2003** \| *‹View›* ➤ *‹Header and Footer›*]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| In *‹Print Layout›* view, double-click
    > near the top of the page.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**WordPerfect** \| If no header exists, add one. *‹Insert›* ➤
    > *‹Header / Footer›*. After adding a header, click in the header
    > box that appears on the page.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**Pages** \| Skip this step. Instead, hold down the command key
    > and click in the corner of the document so there's no active text
    > area.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

5.  Create a tall, narrow text box in the left margin. (For those
    unfamiliar, a *text box* lets you put text in an arbitrary position
    on the page, outside the flow of the main document.)

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**Word 2003** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Text Box›*. Click and drag on the
    > page to create the box.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**Word 2007 & 2010** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Text›* panel ➤ *‹Text
    > Box›* ➤ *‹Draw Text Box›*. Click and drag on the page to create
    > the box.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**WordPerfect** \| *‹Insert›* menu ➤ *‹Text Box›*. A default text
    > box will appear. Adjust the size and position as needed.
    > Right-click the text box and select *‹Wrap›* ➤ *‹Behind
    > Text›*.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[In Word and WordPerfect, the new text box may have an unwanted
    > border. If so, right-click in the text box and use the menu
    > options to remove the border.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [**Pages** \| *‹Insert›* ➤ *‹Text Box›*. A default text box will
    > appear. Adjust the size and position as needed. Select the text
    > box and then select *‹Format›* ➤ *‹Advanced›* ➤ *‹Move Object to
    > Section Master›*.]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

6.  In the text box, type your line numbers. Use a [hard line
    break](#calibre_link-32) between each number, not a [carriage
    return](#calibre_link-33), so the whole set of numbers is one
    paragraph. Select all the numbers. As you did before, use paragraph
    formatting to set the line spacing to exactly 24 points.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

7.  Drag the text box near the H paragraph so it looks approximately
    aligned.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

8.  Zoom in closely to the top left corner of the H paragraph so you can
    see the H paragraph on the right, and the first few numbers in the
    text box on the left. Select the text box and use the arrows on your
    keyboard to move the text box vertically until the bottoms of the
    numbers line up with the bottoms of the lines in the H paragraph.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

That's it. You should now have line numbers that repeat on every page
and that line up with the [body text](#calibre_link-66). Remember ---
all the paragraphs in your document, including headings, must have line
spacing of exactly 24 points and no [space between
paragraphs](#calibre_link-62). Otherwise, the text will no longer align
with the line numbers.

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ The line numbers themselves don't have to be the same [point
size](#calibre_link-46) or font style as the [body
text](#calibre_link-66). I like to make them smaller so they look more
like a background element on the page. (See [maxims of page
layout](#calibre_link-81) for the idea of contrasting foregrounds and
backgrounds.) Make these adjustments to the line numbers before aligning
them with the body text.

::: calibre9
:::

➤ See the [caption page](#calibre_link-83) and
[motion](#calibre_link-84) sample documents for more tips on typography
in court filings.

::: calibre9
:::

::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1598 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1599 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1600 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1601 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1602 .mbppagebreak}
[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[Bates numbering]{.bold1}

[[Try orange Franklin Gothic]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Just as paper is yielding to PDF as the preferred format for discovery,
manually stamped *Bates numbering* is yielding to digital numbering.

::: calibre9
:::

I prefer the convenience of digital Bates numbering. But I miss the look
of the stamp. The blue ink and the distinct font made the number easy to
spot on the page, even after being photocopied. Digital Bates numbering
is often small, black, and set in [Times New Roman](#calibre_link-56) or
Arial --- all of which camouflages the numbers.

::: calibre9
:::

Tips for making digital Bates numbers stand out:

::: calibre9
:::

1.  Make the [point size](#calibre_link-46) reasonably big --- not
    smaller than 12 point.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  Use a font with good numerals. I like Franklin Gothic Medium or
    Franklin Gothic Demi. Avoid fonts that are condensed or dark on the
    page (e.g., Arial Black). These fonts are hard to read at small
    sizes and don't stand up well to scanning or photocopying.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Use color. I use a medium-dark orange. Not the most attractive, but
    it stands out because orange rarely occurs in discovery (unless
    you're suing a carrot company). Orange isn't so bright that it gets
    washed out when reproduced in black and white.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
4.  Make sure your Bates numbers don't get stamped in the content of the
    document. Adobe Acrobat can shrink documents uniformly so there's a
    clean margin for the numbering.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

[How to apply customized Bates numbering]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

> [**Adobe Acrobat** \| *‹Advanced›* ➤ *‹Document Processing›* ➤ *‹Bates
> Numbering›* ➤ *‹Add›*. Select the documents to be numbered and click
> *‹OK›*. You'll be taken to the Acrobat *‹Add Header and Footer›*
> window.]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The middle of this window has six boxes corresponding to locations on
the page (*‹Left Header Text›*, *‹Center Header Text›*, and so on).
Click in the box where you want your Bates number. Click *‹Insert Bates
Number›* and then click *‹OK›*. You will get a dinky, illegible Bates
number.

::: calibre9
:::

Near the top of the window, under the label *‹Font›*, change the font
and the point size. Click the black square to the right to change the
color.

::: calibre9
:::

To ensure your Bates numbers don't collide with any page content, click
*‹Appearance Options›* and check *‹Shrink document to avoid overwriting
the document's text and graphics›*. Click *‹OK›*.

::: calibre9
:::

Make any other necessary adjustments in the main window. Click *‹OK›* to
apply the Bates numbering.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1603 .mbppagebreak}
:::

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[paragraph and character styles]{.bold1}

[[The secret to typographic success]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Implementing good typography is often a chore and a bore. For everyone,
not just for lawyers. *Paragraph and character styles* eliminate most of
the drudgery.

::: calibre9
:::

Styles are the DNA of document layout. Styles make it easy to control
typography across a single document. They also let you make templates
that can be reused across multiple documents. The result is better, more
consistent typography in every document with less work per document.

::: calibre9
:::

I find it curious that so many lawyers don't know how to use styles.
They format their documents the old-fashioned way: word by word and
paragraph by paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

Do you check your spelling by having a human being read your draft? No,
you use an automated spelling checker. Do you copy a document by putting
each page on the photocopier glass? No, you put the whole thing in the
sheet feeder.

::: calibre9
:::

If you plan to have a long-term relationship with good typography, I
recommend you learn how to use styles too.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Word, WordPerfect, and Pages all offer paragraph and character
> styles. They work basically the same way, but the details are quite
> different between the three. I can't give you a detailed tutorial on
> using paragraph and character styles --- you'll need to spend some
> time with your manual or help file. If self-study is too daunting,
> consider software training. Training is a great investment. Your word
> processor is a tool you use every day. If you don't know how to use
> its most powerful features, you're wasting time every
> day.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[Why you should care about styles]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Styles let you *define sets of formatting attributes* that get
    applied together. So instead of selecting a heading, changing it to
    13 point, bold, and all caps, you can define a style that includes
    these three attributes, and apply the style to the heading.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    What's the benefit? When you come across the next heading, you don't
    need to individually apply those three attributes. You apply the
    style you defined before. The headings will then match.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Styles let you *change formatting* across a class of related
    elements. Suppose you want to change your headings from 13 point to
    13.5 point. Instead of selecting each heading separately and
    changing the point size --- a tedious project --- you can change the
    point size in the heading style definition from 13 point to 13.5
    point. Headings using that style will be automatically updated.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    What's the benefit? Updating the formatting is centralized and
    automatic. You can experiment with formatting and layout ideas with
    little manual effort.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Styles can *inherit formatting* from other styles. A change to the
    parent style will propagate to all the substyles. But a change to
    the substyle will only affect that one style.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    What's the benefit? Inheritance adds another layer of centralized
    automation --- it's like having styles of styles. You can define a
    set of foundation styles and use them as the basis for more
    elaborate styles.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

[How to use styles effectively]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

*Character styles* can incorporate attributes of words and sentences,
like font, [point size](#calibre_link-46),
[letterspacing](#calibre_link-49), [bold or italic](#calibre_link-44),
[all caps](#calibre_link-45), and [small caps](#calibre_link-53).

::: calibre9
:::

*Paragraph styles* can incorporate those attributes and also layout
attributes like [line spacing](#calibre_link-63), [first-line
indents](#calibre_link-61), and [rules and borders](#calibre_link-71).

::: calibre9
:::

Why have two kinds of styles? You can only apply one paragraph style to
each paragraph, but you can apply multiple character styles to text
within the paragraph.

::: calibre9
:::

As a rule of thumb, any time you have two document elements that should
be formatted identically, you'll want to use a style.

::: calibre9
:::

Initially, you may be inclined to define styles like "Caslon Bold 11.5
point." That's better than applying the same formatting manually. But it
overlooks another benefit of styles, which is to define formatting in
terms of what each paragraph is used for, rather than how it looks. If
you're creating a style for a block quotation, the name "Caslon Bold
11.5 point" is not as good as "Block Quotation." And later, if you
change the formatting, the name will still be accurate.

::: calibre9
:::

Word processors come with a long list of built-in styles. Word, for
instance, has *‹Heading 1›* through *‹Heading 9›*, *‹Quote›*,
*‹Caption›*, *‹Header›*, *‹Footer›*, and so on. Many of these styles are
wired into other functions. It's good practice to modify the built-in
styles when possible rather than create new ones.

::: calibre9
:::

When you do this, you'll also notice that many built-in styles are
horribly ugly. For example, Word's *‹Header 1›* is 14-point blue
Cambria, a style with no redeeming qualities. I'm not worried that you'd
use it without fixing it first. At this point, you know better.

::: calibre9
:::

[[Page layout](#calibre_link-197) · [Advanced
rules](#calibre_link-197)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[maxims of page layout]{.bold1}

[[Principles of balance & consistency]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Successful typography requires you to pay attention to the whole, not
just the parts. These maxims summarize the key principles I keep in mind
when I'm laying out a document.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  **Decide first how the body text will look.**

    Why? Because there's more [body text](#calibre_link-66) than
    anything else. Four decisions --- [point size](#calibre_link-46),
    [line length](#calibre_link-64), [line spacing](#calibre_link-63),
    and font --- largely determine the appearance of the body text.
    Therefore, these decisions have the biggest influence on the
    legibility of the text and the overall appearance of the document.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  **Divide the page into foreground and background.**

    The foreground contains the most important page elements. (Hint: the
    body text is usually one of them.) The background contains
    everything else. Don't let the background elements upstage the
    foreground elements. And remember that you have a limited number of
    tools for making distinctions: position, size, font, and sometimes
    color. (See [letterhead](#calibre_link-86) for an example of how to
    handle the foreground--background relationship.)

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  **Make adjustments with the smallest visible increments.**

    Typography thrives on fine details. The difference between not
    enough and too much can be small.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

4.  **When in doubt, try it both ways.**

    Don't try to resolve typographic decisions with logic. There's no
    substitute for printing samples of two options and getting a visual
    reaction.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

5.  **Be consistent.**

    Typography quietly describes to readers a structure and hierarchy.
    Things that are the same should look the same. Things that look
    different should actually be different. Without consistent treatment
    of similar elements, the document will feel random and meandering.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

6.  **Relate each new element to existing elements.**

    The only time you have unfettered discretion is when the page is
    blank. After that, the page is like a jigsaw puzzle that becomes
    more constrained with each new piece.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

7.  **Keep it simple.**

    A principle as true in typography as anything else. If you think you
    need three colors and five fonts, think again.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

8.  **Imitate what you like.**

    Why reinvent the wheel? If you see typography you like --- in a
    book, in a magazine, on a sign --- emulate it. Learning to see
    what's good about other examples of typography makes it easier to
    solve problems in your own layouts.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

9.  **Don't fear white space.**

    A lot of mediocre typography results from a perceived need to fill
    space. Things get too big or spread out. Work outward from the text,
    not inward from the page edges. If the text looks good, the white
    space will take care of itself.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

::: {#calibre_link-1656 .mbppagebreak}

[[Sample documents]{.bold1}]{.calibre21}

::: calibre3
:::

Now it's time to put everything together. Like good writing, good
typography isn't determined by elements in isolation, but by the
relationship of those elements and the effect of the whole.

::: calibre9
:::

So far we've looked at typographic rules individually. In this chapter,
we'll look at these rules in the context of some common documents. Your
goal is to get better at seeing typographic flaws in layouts and fixing
them.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000067.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

[[Above: That's what I call a letter. Published in Boston,
1853.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Each sample document is presented in the form of a before-and-after
demonstration: "before" represents a typical layout with the usual bad
habits; "after" represents a layout upgraded with the lessons of this
book.

::: calibre9
:::

All the samples in this chapter were created with good old Microsoft
Word 2007 --- no special trickery was used. The names and situations in
the sample documents are, of course, fictitious.

::: calibre9
:::

You can download PDF versions of these sample documents from the web if
you want to study them more carefully. Go to
<http://www.typographyforlawyers.com/code/> and enter the numerical code
listed beneath the sample.

::: calibre9
:::

[caption pages]{.bold1}

[[Use a table for best results]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

If this book were a law-school class, reformatting a caption page could
be the final exam. Caption pages pack a lot of typographic issues into a
small area. How many can you spot?

::: calibre9

:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1771 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1772 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1773 .mbppagebreak}
[Before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  [Monospaced font](#calibre_link-42).\
    Too much text in [all caps](#calibre_link-45).\
    No [letterspacing](#calibre_link-49) of caps.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  Unnecessary [underlining](#calibre_link-40).\
    [Line spacing](#calibre_link-63) too tight.\
    [Rules and borders](#calibre_link-71) made out of punctuation.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Vertical rules too thick.\
    [First-line indent](#calibre_link-61) is too large.\
    [Line numbers](#calibre_link-78) don't align with [body
    text](#calibre_link-66).\
    Inconsistent capitalization in [heading](#calibre_link-47).\
    Awkward line break in heading.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

> [[Despite its appearance, this example did not come from 1957. It's a
> nearly exact replica of a document filed in 2010 by the U.S.
> government in a criminal case I was working on. The names, of course,
> have been changed.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The best way to lay out a caption page is with a
[table](#calibre_link-70). At minimum, the party names and title should
go in a two-column table, like so:

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000069.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

You can also start the table at the top of the page and put the
attorneys and court name in it too. That's how I prefer to do it --- the
table makes it easy to adjust the position of each element. To make
cells spanning two columns, add new rows and then merge the cells in
each row.

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000070.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

The text at the bottom of the page starts outside the table.

::: calibre9
:::

Call me a scofflaw, but I don't align the text in the top half of the
page to the line numbers. It's possible, but it takes a lot of fiddling
and often results in less legible text.

::: calibre9
:::

::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-227 .calibre}

:::::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1825 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1827 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1828 .mbppagebreak}

::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1830 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1831 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1832 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1833 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::: {#calibre_link-1834 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::: {#calibre_link-1835 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::: {#calibre_link-1836 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::: {#calibre_link-1837 .mbppagebreak}
::::::: {#calibre_link-1838 .mbppagebreak}
[After]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Monospaced font replaced with Stempel Garamond.\
    All caps replaced with bold, or nothing. ([Small
    caps](#calibre_link-53) would be another option.)\
    Address lines separated with [hard line breaks](#calibre_link-32).\
    Letterspacing added to court name.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[In the address block, notice that surplus words (e.g.,
    > "Telephone," "E-mail") have been deleted. Cutting unnecessary
    > words will always make your text easier to read. This is
    > especially true when you need to fit a lot of data in a small
    > space. (See also [business
    > cards](#calibre_link-87).)]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Underlining removed.\
    Line spacing looser in top half of page.\
    Rules and borders made with table-cell borders.\
    [Point size](#calibre_link-46) of party names and title slightly
    bigger than other text.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  First-line indent reduced.\
    Line numbers align with body text.\
    Capitalization in heading fixed.\
    Hard line break inserted in heading before "on."

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Recall the second [maxim of page layout](#calibre_link-81) --- *divide
the page into foreground and background*. The vertical rules on a
caption page should seem like part of the background --- they should not
be darker or more prominent than the body text in the foreground.
Removing unnecessary rules will make the body text area feel less
cramped. I use as few as possible. In this revised version, I removed
the vertical rule on the right and one on the left. I made the remaining
rule thinner and moved it away from the text.

::: calibre9
:::

Court filings have ugly typography as a matter of habit, not
requirement. Court rules can be strict, but there's still plenty of room
for good typography (see [how to interpret court
rules](#calibre_link-90)).

::: calibre9
:::

[[Sample documents](#calibre_link-223)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[motions]{.bold1}

[[Extra attention to legibility is time well
spent]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

As a litigator, I understand that motions are often written under
deadline pressure that make good typography seem like an unaffordable
luxury.

::: calibre9
:::

But when is it more important to have your reader's full attention?
You're asking a judge to order a remedy --- or, if you're opposing, to
refrain from ordering that remedy. The issue is important enough to have
reached the judge's desk. The ruling may not be appealable. Shouldn't
you put your best foot forward?

::: calibre9
:::

"Yes, but where do I find the time?" You think about typography *before*
you write your motion --- by setting up a motion template with
[paragraph and character styles](#calibre_link-80) that handle most of
the typography chores as you write. When you get to the end, there's not
much left to do.

::: calibre9
:::

Or you can submit motions that look like they just rolled out of bed.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-1873 .mbppagebreak}

[Before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Top level of [headings](#calibre_link-47) wasted with section
    label.\
    No [letterspacing](#calibre_link-49) in all-caps heading.\
    [Underlining](#calibre_link-40) throughout.\
    Arial used for body text and headings.\
    Word by word capitalization of subheadings.\
    Two spaces between sentences.\
    No [hyphenation](#calibre_link-67) in [justified
    text](#calibre_link-60).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  [Line length](#calibre_link-64) too wide and text too close to
    vertical rules.\
    [Line numbers](#calibre_link-78) and [body text](#calibre_link-66)
    misaligned.\
    Headings are not substantive.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Last heading breaks onto next page.\
    Hyphens used next to page number instead of dashes.\
    [Point size](#calibre_link-46) of footer is too large.\
    No letterspacing in footer text.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

:::::: {#calibre_link-2018 .mbppagebreak}
[After]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Letterspacing added to section label.\
    Underlining removed.\
    Arial replaced with Goudy Old Style.\
    Subheadings promoted to main headings. Capitalization corrected.\
    One space between sentences.\
    Text is justified and hyphenated.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[You might also notice that I've cut a couple words and
    > sentences. Is that cheating? Editing text to improve typography is
    > a technique as old as printing itself. If you're a ruthless
    > editor, you'll find it easier to be an effective
    > typographer.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Line length narrowed; text farther from vertical rules.\
    Line numbers aligned with body text.\
    Headings rewritten to be substantive.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Em dashes used next to page number.\
    Footer point size reduced.\
    Letterspacing added to footer.\
    Last heading moved to next page using [keep lines
    together](#calibre_link-73).

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Notice how much white space has been added around the edges of the text
and near the headings. Remember the ninth [maxim of page
layout](#calibre_link-81) --- *don't fear white space*. You needn't fill
up every square inch permitted by law. Yes, adding white space will
lengthen the document slightly, because you're using less space per
page. The benefit is better typography and better legibility. (If you
genuinely prefer the "Before" motion, it's time to pass this book along
to a friend.)

::: calibre9
:::

:::::::::: {#calibre_link-2057 .mbppagebreak}
[[Sample documents](#calibre_link-223)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[research memos]{.bold1}

[[Bigger margins, smaller point size, tighter line
spacing]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The problems that afflict research memos also afflict other long
documents like settlement agreements and contracts. You can adapt this
recipe for any of them.

::: calibre9
:::

My legal-writing teacher in law school required memos to be formatted in
what I would call the classic typewriter layout --- one-inch margins on
all sides, 12-point font, and double-spaced lines. Because of its
genesis in typewritten documents, this format is the basis of many
institutional document-layout rules. For instance, most courts require
filings to be in some variation of this format.

::: calibre9
:::

But have you ever seen a book, newspaper, or magazine that uses this
layout? No. Why not? Because it's not optimally legible. So why would
anyone use it? Because it suits the severely limited capabilities of the
typewriter. So if we don't use typewriters anymore, why does everyone
still use this layout?

::: calibre9
:::

My thoughts exactly.

::: calibre9
:::

[Before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  [Page margins](#calibre_link-65) too small.\
    [Line length](#calibre_link-64) too wide.\
    [Point size](#calibre_link-46) too big.\
    Two spaces between sentences.\
    [First-line indents](#calibre_link-61) too deep.\
    [Line spacing](#calibre_link-63) too tall.\
    [Justified text](#calibre_link-60) without
    [hyphenation](#calibre_link-67).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  [Underlining](#calibre_link-40) in heading.\
    Headings don't align horizontally with paragraphs.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Hyphens missing from phrasal adjectives.\
    Hyphen used instead of en dash in citation.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Transplanting this document from the 1890s into the present is simple
surgery.

::: calibre9

::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2220 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2221 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2222 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2223 .mbppagebreak}
[After]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Page margins larger (2″ on sides and 1.5″ on top and bottom).\
    Point size smaller.\
    One space between sentences.\
    First-line indents reduced.\
    Line spacing reduced.\
    Hyphenation turned on.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[Note the combination of right-aligned and left-aligned
    > [tabs](#calibre_link-31) in the first four lines, so all eight
    > pieces of text look like they're positioned against an invisible
    > rectangle. If you have time for them, subtle details make a
    > difference.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Line length shorter (about 65 characters per line).\
    No underlining.\
    Headings align with paragraphs.\
    Headings half a point larger than body text.\
    Space added before and after headings.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Hyphens added to phrasal adjectives.\
    En dash in citation.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

An additional virtue: the revised layout fits more text on the page.

::: calibre9
:::

[Sharing draft documents]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

If you're working on documents with other lawyers, you have less
typographic control and should adjust accordingly.

::: calibre9
:::

The major problem is font choice. If you pick a font your collaborator
doesn't have, your collaborator won't see the formatting accurately.

::: calibre9
:::

This is one of the few situations where [system fonts](#calibre_link-43)
are your best choice. Your collaborators are likely to have them, and
these fonts look good on screen, where much of the collaboration
happens. If you like, you can reformat with a different font at the end.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Even with system fonts, perfect visual fidelity is not guaranteed.
> This is one reason some lawyers, me included, like to use numbered
> paragraphs when working on documents with other lawyers --- it
> provides an unambiguous reference system.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

If it's critical that your document appear the same way on your
collaborator's screen as it does on yours, the only foolproof technique
is to share PDF files and use commenting and review tools on the PDF.

::: calibre9
:::

[[Sample documents](#calibre_link-223)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[letterhead]{.bold1}

[[Foreground in front, background in back]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Not everything in a page layout is equally important. As I mentioned in
[maxims of page layout](#calibre_link-81), I think of documents as
having a foreground, containing the most important elements, and a
background, containing everything else. Typography communicates this
distinction to the reader visually.

::: calibre9
:::

Picture a sheet of letterhead. What's in the foreground? If you said
"the address block," then I'm guessing you pictured a blank sheet of
letterhead. But letterhead is never used blank. So more accurately, the
foreground contains the text of the letter. The background contains the
address block.

::: calibre9
:::

Yet lawyer letterhead often suffers from two problems. First, the
address block (the background) dominates the page, upstaging the text of
the letter (the foreground). Second, the foreground and background don't
relate to each other visually.

::: calibre9
:::

[Before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Too much space wasted in top margin.\
    Too much [centered text](#calibre_link-59).\
    Long name set in a wide font.\
    Redundant and unnecessary words.\
    Address block dense and hard to read.\
    Address block too large compared to body text.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  Second office address and date positioned arbitrarily.\
    [Line length](#calibre_link-64) too wide.\
    Left and right [page margins](#calibre_link-65) too small.\
    [First-line indents](#calibre_link-61) used with [space between
    paragraphs](#calibre_link-62).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Signature block positioned arbitrarily.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

This letterhead can be improved by making the text of the letter more
prominent, reducing the weight of the address block, and making the
overall layout less disjointed.

::: calibre9
:::

[After]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Top margin smaller.\
    No more centered text.\
    Unnecessary words removed.\
    Address block set in lighter, more legible font.\
    Horizontal rule same width as text of letter.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[Setting the address block in gray or another color would also
    > help keep it part of the background.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  Address lines separated with [hard line breaks](#calibre_link-32).\
    Letter paragraphs align to left side.\
    Letter starts higher on the page.\
    Line length narrower; left and right page margins larger.\
    First-line indents removed.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  Second office address used as footer.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

The address block at the top is set up with a [table](#calibre_link-70):

::: calibre9
:::

![](images/000078.jpg){.calibre22}

::: calibre9
:::

Notice how the horizontal rules in the header and footer define a
rectangle that all the page elements relate to in some way, improving
the overall cohesion of the layout.

::: calibre9
:::

[Production tips for letterhead]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

The finest letterhead comes from *letterpress printers*, who use
old-fashioned metal type. Every major city supports at least a couple of
letterpress printers. Most of their business comes from wedding
invitations and stationery. It's more expensive than other methods, but
the results are nonpareil.

::: calibre9
:::

Next up are *offset printers.* (*Offset* is short for *offset
lithography*, the process used to make 99% of printed goods.) Offset
printers range from high-end commercial outfits to tiny neighborhood
shops. I'd like to assure you that price and quality correlate, but they
don't. I've worked with neighborhood shops that have done a great job,
and big printers that have seriously goofed. Ask a colleague to
recommend a printer. If the work isn't right, ask to have it reprinted.

::: calibre9
:::

Many offset printers offer graphic-design services as a convenience to
their customers, much the same way that bowling alleys rent shoes. These
design services are usually fine unless you want the finished work to
contain more than a modicum of originality or finesse. In that case,
hire an independent graphic designer. (More on that below.)

::: calibre9
:::

> [[You don't need a graphic designer if you hire a letterpress printer.
> Letterpress printers are usually limited to the fonts they have on
> hand, so they're in the best position to handle the design work. But
> it is possible to convert digital files to plates that can be printed
> by letterpress.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

What about Internet offset printers? (Meaning, websites where you upload
a PDF, which is then printed and shipped back to you in a week or so.)
I've been pleasantly surprised by the quality of their work. I can
recommend them for jobs where you need a small print run (e.g., less
than 500 pieces) and a custom offset-printing job wouldn't be
economical.

::: calibre9
:::

Internet printers keep their prices low by combining multiple print jobs
into one. This means your paper choices are limited. Also, every print
job is done in *process color*, which involves four basic ink colors ---
cyan, magenta, yellow, black --- being combined to simulate other
colors. Most commercial full-color printing is done using process color.
But for stationery, it's usually best to use *spot color*, where each
color gets its own print run. Colors printed with process color will
contain a noticeable dot pattern; colors printed with spot color will
not. This dot pattern can also make small text printed in process colors
look gritty. The only safe color is black.

::: calibre9
:::

The cheapest option is to make letterhead yourself with your laser
printer. If you think that would be anathema to a typography snob like
me, think again. In fact, I'm not ashamed to admit it --- I only use
laser-printed letterhead.

::: calibre9
:::

Why? My law practice consists of one lawyer who never mails a letter if
an e-mail or PDF will suffice. I use so little letterhead that it's
never been economical to have it professionally printed. I imagine that
describes an increasing number of law offices. So for them, some tips.

::: calibre9
:::

Laser-printed letterhead often looks flat and cheap. Therefore, you must
overcome the three telltale signs of laser-printed letterhead.

::: calibre9
:::

1.  *The typography is terrible*. That's been covered above --- take the
    same care with your letterhead typography that you would if you were
    going to spend \$5,000 printing it.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

2.  *It's printed on basic white printer paper*. Splurge on some deluxe
    paper at your local specialty-paper store. (I use Crane's Crest
    cotton paper. It's not cheap.) Get off-white or ivory paper --- pure
    white tends to highlight flaws in the laser printing. Choose *wove*
    paper, which is smooth, rather than *laid* paper, which has a ribbed
    texture. Laser toner affixes better to a smooth surface. (More about
    this in [printers and paper](#calibre_link-91).)

    ::: calibre9
    :::

    > [[Dedicated paper stores like Kelly Paper
    > ([www.kellypaper.com](http://www.kellypaper.com)) stock the best
    > selection of laser-printable letterhead paper. General
    > office-supply stores don't usually carry the good
    > stuff.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

    ::: calibre9
    :::

3.  *The name and address are printed in black*. Compared to black
    printing ink, black laser toner has a characteristic luster, and is
    usually closer to dark gray than black. Heighten the illusion by
    printing the name and address in a color --- something pale and
    noncontroversial. Color laser printers also use process color, so
    run tests to make sure the color you pick doesn't have a gritty dot
    pattern. Grayish-blue tones often work well.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

[Working with a graphic designer]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

Graphic designers are everywhere, at every price point. As with offset
printers, I wish I could say that price and quality correlate, but they
don't.

::: calibre9
:::

The most common error made by people hiring graphic designers is
devoting too little time to selecting the designer, and too much time to
critiquing the design work. Instead, spend all the time you want
choosing a designer. But once you choose, get out of the way and let the
designer do their thing. You'll get better results.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[This is an error clients make when selecting lawyers, too. If you
> don't like bossy clients, don't be one.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

These days, any graphic designer worth their salt has an online
portfolio. Reviewing online portfolios is the easiest way to find
potential designers.

::: calibre9
:::

It's fine to ask a graphic designer to show you samples of work they've
done for similar clients. It's also fine to ask for a detailed proposal
with deliverables and budget. But I recommend getting detailed proposals
from only two designers, and at most three --- after that you'll be
hopelessly confused.

::: calibre9
:::

It's not fine to ask a graphic designer to work for free, or for a
discount, or on spec. If you don't like the fee, you can always find
someone cheaper. Creative professionals often need more legal advice
than they can afford, so barter may be an option.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[If you're worried about paying for work you don't like, put approval
> milestones in the contract that give you the option to terminate for a
> pro rata fee.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Beyond the design advantages, a graphic designer will often know of good
local printers and will work with the printer to get the job done --- so
you don't have to.

::: calibre9
:::

If you hire a graphic designer to make your letterhead, always test
sample designs by printing out a real letter, or have the designer mock
one up. You can't decide on letterhead just by looking at the name and
address block in isolation. As these examples illustrate, everything has
to work together.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Consensus kills. If you work at a big firm, approval authority for
> creative work should be vested in a small group of people --- four at
> most, with a designated leader. You'll never please everyone, so
> there's no point in trying.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[[[--- by the way ---]{.calibre18}]{.bold1}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

➤ Lawyers typically live up to their reputation of being risk-averse. So
why do some big firms use letterhead that lists all their partners (and
sometimes also their associates)? If a lawyer joins the firm, the
letterhead has to be reprinted. If a lawyer leaves the firm, the
letterhead has to be reprinted. While the firm awaits the new
letterhead, the current letterhead lists a departed attorney, which is
possibly an ethics violation. (See, e.g., ABA Model Rules of Prof.
Conduct 7.5 and 7.1; Calif. Rule of Prof. Conduct 1-400(A)(2).)
Furthermore, all those names waste a lot of space and usually have to be
printed so small that they're barely legible. Bottom line: bad idea.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-2454 .mbppagebreak}

[[Sample documents](#calibre_link-223)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[business cards]{.bold1}

[[Shrink and simplify]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

Business cards, like [caption pages](#calibre_link-83), have to fit a
lot of information in a small area. But they often try to do too much.

::: calibre9
:::

For instance, the card layout below is fairly common among lawyers. I
call it the baseball-diamond layout: information is pushed out to the
corners, and your eye has to travel around the edge of the whole card to
read everything.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-2604 .mbppagebreak}

[Before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

[Goofy font](#calibre_link-41) used for name.\
Copperplate font used for other text.\
[Point size](#calibre_link-46) of name too large.\
No [letterspacing](#calibre_link-49) in caps.\
Information pushed out to corners.

::: calibre9
:::

The guiding principles with business cards are the same as with
[letterhead](#calibre_link-86). Remove anything nonessential. Don't
worry about the text being small --- there's not very much of it. Build
the layout from the text outward. The white space will take care of
itself. If you work from the edges of the card inward, you're more
likely to end up with a baseball diamond.

::: calibre9
:::

[After]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

One non-goofy font used for all text.\
Point size more reasonable and consistent.\
Text layout simplified.\
Pomposity eliminated.

::: calibre9
:::

[Production tips for business cards]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

See the notes under [letterhead](#calibre_link-86) for general tips
about getting stationery items designed and printed.

::: calibre9
:::

In addition to those tips, carefully consider the paper stock for your
business cards. More than other printed items, business cards provide a
tactile experience, much like shaking someone's hand. A business card
should feel great between your fingers. I've gotten too many cards from
lawyers that felt like valet-parking receipts.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Color can be a nice touch on business cards, but it has to be
> understated. The louder the color, the less of it you can use, and
> vice versa.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

For that reason, I can't endorse laser-printed business cards. The
sheets of perforated cards designed for laser printing are made of thin
and cheap paper, and the resulting business cards are flimsy and sad.
Get them professionally printed.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[My recommendations for business-card printing services are on the
> Typography for Lawyers website. Use code
> [19302](http://typo.la/19302).]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-2809 .mbppagebreak}

:::::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-250 .calibre}
[[Sample documents](#calibre_link-223)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

[résumés]{.bold1}

[[Avoid dense text by using a second page]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

During law school, I interviewed for a job at a small law firm. One of
the hiring partner's first comments was "It's so unusual that I see a
résumé without any typos."

::: calibre9
:::

"Are you serious?" I said.

::: calibre9
:::

She said, "Yes, probably 90% of the résumés I get have typos. And that
includes the ones we get from the top schools."

::: calibre9
:::

I got the job. Probably there were better-qualified candidates, but they
damaged their chances with sloppy résumés. The irony is that those
people, who most needed to hear this hiring partner's feedback, weren't
in the room. Because they never got an interview.

::: calibre9
:::

Consider yourself warned.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[This is a book on typography, not typos, but nothing beats having
> another person --- or even better, more than one --- edit your résumé.
> Insist that each reviewer propose at least three
> edits.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

The biggest problem I see with résumés is that they are uncomfortably
dense with text. I take this to be the influence of the myth that a
résumé can only be one page long.

::: calibre9
:::

Unless a potential employer demands one page, feel free to make your
résumé two pages. Or longer, if necessary. This will ease your
typographic problems. Two caveats, however. Don't assume a reader will
get past the first page --- put the most important information up front.
And when I say "if necessary," remember you're writing for a potential
employer, not your mom. My résumé fits on two pages. I'll bet yours can
too.

::: calibre9
:::

> [[**Résumé** is the original spelling and still preferred. **Resumé**
> is acceptable. **Resume** is common but wrong. See the table on the
> inside back cover to learn how to type the é
> character.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

> [[Print a two-page résumé with two sheets of paper, even if you have a
> duplex printer. You don't want a potential employer to overlook the
> second page because it's printed on the back of the
> first.]{.calibre18}]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::::: {#calibre_link-2810 .mbppagebreak}
[![](images/000082.jpg){.calibre22}[Code
19501](http://typo.la/19501)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-2811 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::
::::::::::::::::::

::::::: {#calibre_link-251 .calibre}
:::::: {#calibre_link-2812 .mbppagebreak}
::::: {#calibre_link-2813 .mbppagebreak}
[Before]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  [Page margins](#calibre_link-65) too small; [line
    length](#calibre_link-64) too big.\
    All text set in Calibri (a [system font](#calibre_link-43)).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  Headings and gray boxes are too large relative to body text.\
    Key information --- the where and when --- is buried.\
    Ugly [bulleted lists](#calibre_link-69).

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Too much information on one page.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

::: {#calibre_link-2814 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::
::::::
:::::::

:::::::: {#calibre_link-252 .calibre}
::::::: {#calibre_link-2815 .mbppagebreak}
:::::: {#calibre_link-2816 .mbppagebreak}
::::: {#calibre_link-2817 .mbppagebreak}
[![](images/000083.jpg){.calibre22}[Code
19701](http://typo.la/19701)]{.calibre7}

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-2818 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::
::::::
:::::::
::::::::

::::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-253 .calibre}
:::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2819 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2820 .mbppagebreak}
:::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2821 .mbppagebreak}
::::::::::: {#calibre_link-2822 .mbppagebreak}
[After]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

1.  Page margins bigger; line length smaller.\
    No more Calibri.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
2.  Headings are smaller and lighter.\
    Names of schools and businesses are larger.\
    Gentler list bullets.

    ::: calibre9
    :::
3.  Nonessential information moved to second page.

    ::: calibre9
    :::

Keep in mind that one way a résumé illustrates your virtues is by
drawing connections between you --- whom the reader knows nothing about
--- and various schools and employers, which the reader may have heard
of. The implied syllogism goes like this: *Falkenburg, Fester & Funk is
an elite firm; this person worked at Falkenburg; therefore, this person
is an elite-quality lawyer*. Don't make your reader struggle to dig out
the names of those schools and employers --- make sure they're
immediately visible.

::: calibre9
:::

In this revision, the visual emphasis has shifted from the headings ---
who cares about résumé headings? --- to the substance. This also makes
the résumé more skimmable, which is never a bad thing.

::: calibre9
:::

[Production tips for résumés]{.bold1}

::: calibre17
:::

I treat a résumé as a special kind of laser-printed
[letterhead](#calibre_link-86). Review those [production
tips](#calibre_link-254).

::: calibre9
:::

Past that, resist the urge to buy paper specially marketed for résumés
--- the kinds that come in odd colors (e.g., green, pink, gray) or
textures (e.g., parchment, marble, linen). High-quality
business-letterhead paper from the stationery store is just fine.
Anything more elaborate looks overbaked.

::: calibre9
:::

Increasingly, employers and recruiters are asking for résumés in PDF
format. In PDF, good typography survives; good paper is irrelevant.

::: calibre9
:::

::: {#calibre_link-2823 .mbppagebreak}
:::
:::::::::::
::::::::::::
:::::::::::::
::::::::::::::
:::::::::::::::

:::::::::: {#calibre_link-255 .calibre}
::::::::: {#calibre_link-2824 .mbppagebreak}

::::::: {#calibre_link-2826 .mbppagebreak}
:::::: {#calibre_link-2827 .mbppagebreak}
::::: {#calibre_link-2828 .mbppagebreak}
